<p><b>Benazir Bhutto</b> (<a href="/wiki/Sindhi_language" title="Sindhi language">Sindhi</a>: <span lang="sd" dir="rtl" xml:lang="sd">بينظير ڀُٽو</span>‎; 21 June 1953 – 27 December 2007) was a Pakistani politician who served as <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Pakistan" title="Prime Minister of Pakistan">Prime Minister of Pakistan</a> from 1988 to 1990 and again from 1993 to 1996. She was the first woman to head a democratic government in a <a href="/wiki/Muslim_majority_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="Muslim majority countries">Muslim majority</a> nation. Ideologically a <a href="/wiki/Liberalism" title="Liberalism">liberal</a> and a <a href="/wiki/Secularism" title="Secularism">secularist</a>, she chaired or co-chaired the <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Peoples_Party" title="Pakistan Peoples Party">Pakistan Peoples Party</a> (PPP) from the early 1980s until her death.</p>
<p>Bhutto was born in <a href="/wiki/Karachi" title="Karachi">Karachi</a> to a <a href="/wiki/Bhutto_family" title="Bhutto family">politically important, aristocratic family</a>; her father, the PPP's founder and leader <a href="/wiki/Zulfikar_Ali_Bhutto" title="Zulfikar Ali Bhutto">Zulfikar</a>, was elected Prime Minister on a <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialist</a> platform in 1973. Bhutto studied at <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a> and the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a>, where she was President of the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Union" title="Oxford Union">Oxford Union</a>. She returned to Pakistan, where her father was <a href="/wiki/Operation_Fair_Play" title="Operation Fair Play">ousted in a 1977 military coup</a> and executed. Bhutto and her mother <a href="/wiki/Nusrat_Bhutto" title="Nusrat Bhutto">Nusrat</a> took control of the PPP and led the country's <a href="/wiki/Movement_for_the_Restoration_of_Democracy" title="Movement for the Restoration of Democracy">Movement for the Restoration of Democracy</a>; Bhutto was repeatedly imprisoned by <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Zia-ul-Haq" title="Muhammad Zia-ul-Haq">Muhammad Zia-ul-Haq</a>'s military government and then exiled to Britain in 1984. She returned in 1986 and transformed the PPP's platform from a socialist to a liberal one, before leading it to victory in the <a href="/wiki/Pakistani_general_election,_1988" title="Pakistani general election, 1988">1988 election</a>. As Prime Minister, her attempts at reform were stifled by <a href="/wiki/Conservatism" title="Conservatism">conservative</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamist</a> forces, including President <a href="/wiki/Ghulam_Ishaq_Khan" title="Ghulam Ishaq Khan">Ghulam Ishaq Khan</a> and the powerful military. Her administration was accused of corruption and nepotism, and dismissed by Khan in 1990. Intelligence services rigged the <a href="/wiki/Pakistani_general_election,_1990" title="Pakistani general election, 1990">1990 election</a> to ensure a victory for the conservative <a href="/wiki/Islami_Jamhoori_Ittehad" title="Islami Jamhoori Ittehad">Islami Jamhoori Ittehad</a> (IJI), after which Bhutto served as the <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Opposition_(Pakistan)" title="Leader of the Opposition (Pakistan)">Leader of the Opposition</a>.</p>
<p>After the IJI government of Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Nawaz_Sharif" title="Nawaz Sharif">Nawaz Sharif</a> was also dismissed on corruption charges, Bhutto led the PPP to victory in the <a href="/wiki/Pakistani_general_election,_1993" title="Pakistani general election, 1993">1993 elections</a>. Her second term oversaw economic privatisation and attempts to advance <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women's rights">women's rights</a>. Her government was damaged by several controversies, including the assassination of her brother <a href="/wiki/Murtaza_Bhutto" title="Murtaza Bhutto">Murtaza</a>, a <a href="/wiki/1995_Pakistani_coup_d%27%C3%A9tat_attempt" title="1995 Pakistani coup d'état attempt">failed 1995 coup d'état</a>, and a further bribery scandal involving her and her husband <a href="/wiki/Asif_Ali_Zardari" title="Asif Ali Zardari">Asif Ali Zardari</a>; in response to the latter, the President again dismissed her government. The PPP lost the <a href="/wiki/Pakistani_general_election,_1997" title="Pakistani general election, 1997">1997 election</a> and in 1998 she went into self-exile in <a href="/wiki/Dubai" title="Dubai">Dubai</a>, leading her party mainly through proxies. A widening corruption inquiry culminated in a 2003 conviction in a Swiss court. Following United States-brokered negotiations with President <a href="/wiki/Pervez_Musharraf" title="Pervez Musharraf">Pervez Musharraf</a>, she returned to Pakistan in 2007 to compete in the <a href="/wiki/Pakistani_general_election,_2008" title="Pakistani general election, 2008">2008 elections</a>; her platform emphasised civilian oversight of the military and opposition to growing Islamist violence. After a political rally in <a href="/wiki/Rawalpindi" title="Rawalpindi">Rawalpindi</a>, she was assassinated by a <a href="/wiki/Suicide_bomber" class="mw-redirect" title="Suicide bomber">suicide bomber</a>; the militant Islamist group <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">al-Qaeda</a> claimed responsibility, although the involvement of the <a href="/wiki/Pakistani_Taliban" class="mw-redirect" title="Pakistani Taliban">Pakistani Taliban</a> and rogue elements of the intelligence services were widely suspected. She was buried at her <a href="/wiki/Bhutto_family_mausoleum" title="Bhutto family mausoleum">family mausoleum</a>.</p>
<p>Bhutto was a controversial figure. She was often criticised as being politically inexperienced and <a href="/wiki/Corruption_charges_against_Benazir_Bhutto_and_Asif_Ali_Zardari" title="Corruption charges against Benazir Bhutto and Asif Ali Zardari">corrupt</a>, and faced much opposition from Pakistan's Islamist lobby for her secularist and modernising agenda. She nevertheless remained domestically popular and also attracted support from Western nations, for whom she was a champion of democracy and women's rights. Several <a href="/wiki/Benazir_Bhutto_University_(disambiguation)" class="mw-redirect mw-disambig" title="Benazir Bhutto University (disambiguation)">universities and public buildings</a> in Pakistan bear Benazir's name, while her career influenced a number of activists including <a href="/wiki/Malala_Yousafzai" title="Malala Yousafzai">Malala Yousafzai</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Reporter_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Reporter-1">[1]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-tribune.com.pk_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tribune.com.pk-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Early life">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Childhood:_1953–68"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Childhood:_1953.E2.80.9368">Childhood: 1953–68</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Childhood: 1953–68">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Bhutto_family" title="Bhutto family">Bhutto family</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Zulfikar_Ali_Bhutto.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Zulfikar_Ali_Bhutto.jpg/170px-Zulfikar_Ali_Bhutto.jpg" width="170" height="205" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Zulfikar_Ali_Bhutto.jpg/255px-Zulfikar_Ali_Bhutto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/Zulfikar_Ali_Bhutto.jpg/340px-Zulfikar_Ali_Bhutto.jpg 2x" data-file-width="405" data-file-height="489" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Zulfikar_Ali_Bhutto.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Zulfikar_Ali_Bhutto" title="Zulfikar Ali Bhutto">Zulfikar Ali Bhutto</a>, Benazir's father, was Prime Minister of Pakistan and founding chairman of the PPP.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Bhutto was born on 21 June 1953 in the Pakistani city of <a href="/wiki/Karachi" title="Karachi">Karachi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200814KhuhroSoomro2013274_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200814KhuhroSoomro2013274-3">[3]</a></sup> Her father was the politician <a href="/wiki/Zulfikar_Ali_Bhutto" title="Zulfikar Ali Bhutto">Zulfikar Ali Bhutto</a><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013274_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013274-4">[4]</a></sup> and her mother was <a href="/wiki/Nusrat_Bhutto" title="Nusrat Bhutto">Begum Nusrat Ispahani</a>, of Iranian <a href="/wiki/Kurdish_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Kurdish people">Kurdish</a> descent.<sup id="cite_ref-Kurd_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kurd-5">[5]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> Zulfikar was the son of <a href="/wiki/Shah_Nawaz_Bhutto" title="Shah Nawaz Bhutto">Shah Nawaz Bhutto</a>, a prominent politician who had served as Prime Minister of the <a href="/wiki/Junagadh_State" title="Junagadh State">Junagadh State</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200812.E2.80.9313Mu.C3.B1oz201320_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200812.E2.80.9313Mu.C3.B1oz201320-8">[8]</a></sup> The Bhuttos were aristocratic,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199129_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199129-9">[9]</a></sup> wealthy landlords from <a href="/wiki/Sindh" title="Sindh">Sindh</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008x_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008x-10">[10]</a></sup> and were <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni Muslims</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200812_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200812-11">[11]</a></sup> She was their first child,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200814Mu.C3.B1oz201319_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200814Mu.C3.B1oz201319-12">[12]</a></sup> and following Benazir's birth, her parents had three further children: <a href="/wiki/Murtaza_Bhutto" title="Murtaza Bhutto">Murtaza</a> was born in 1954, Sanam in 1957, and <a href="/wiki/Shahnawaz_Bhutto" title="Shahnawaz Bhutto">Shahnawaz</a> in 1958.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200814_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200814-13">[13]</a></sup> Her first language was <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>; as a child she spoke <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a> less frequently, and barely spoke the local <a href="/wiki/Sindhi_language" title="Sindhi language">Sindhi language</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201319_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201319-14">[14]</a></sup></p>
<p>Benazir initially attended the Lady Jennings Nursery School in Karachi.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200814_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200814-13">[13]</a></sup> She was then sent to the <a href="/wiki/Convent_of_Jesus_and_Mary_(Karachi)" class="mw-redirect" title="Convent of Jesus and Mary (Karachi)">Convent of Jesus and Mary</a> in Karachi and from there to the <a href="/wiki/Convent_of_Jesus_and_Mary_(Murree)" class="mw-redirect" title="Convent of Jesus and Mary (Murree)">Jesus and Mary Convent</a>, a boarding school in <a href="/wiki/Murree" title="Murree">Murree</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200813.2C_14_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200813.2C_14-15">[15]</a></sup> Murree is near the border with India, and during the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Pakistani_War_of_1965" title="Indo-Pakistani War of 1965">Indo-Pakistani War of 1965</a> Bhutto and the other pupils underwent air-raid practices.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200860_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200860-16">[16]</a></sup> Bhutto passed her <a href="/wiki/O-levels" class="mw-redirect" title="O-levels">O-levels</a> with high grades.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200814_13-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200814-13">[13]</a></sup> She then completed her <a href="/wiki/A-Level" class="mw-redirect" title="A-Level">A-Levels</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Karachi_Grammar_School" title="Karachi Grammar School">Karachi Grammar School</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>Throughout her youth, Bhutto idolised her father,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200816_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200816-17">[17]</a></sup> and he in turn encouraged her educational development in contravention of traditional approaches to women then pervasive in Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200814_13-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200814-13">[13]</a></sup> When Bhutto was five, her father became the cabinet minister for energy, and when she was nine he became the country's foreign minister.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200813_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200813-18">[18]</a></sup> When she was thirteen, he resigned from the government and a year later established his own political party, the <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_People%27s_Party" class="mw-redirect" title="Pakistan People's Party">Pakistan People's Party</a> (PPP).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200813Mu.C3.B1oz201342_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200813Mu.C3.B1oz201342-19">[19]</a></sup> Bhutto immediately joined.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200813_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200813-18">[18]</a></sup> In 1968, he was arrested and imprisoned but continued to write to her.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200814_13-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200814-13">[13]</a></sup> From an early age, she was exposed to foreign diplomats and figures who were visiting her father, among them <a href="/wiki/Zhou_Enlai" title="Zhou Enlai">Zhou Enlai</a>, <a href="/wiki/Henry_Kissinger" title="Henry Kissinger">Henry Kissinger</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hubert_Humphrey" title="Hubert Humphrey">Hubert Humphrey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200815_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200815-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="University_studies:_1969–77"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="University_studies:_1969.E2.80.9377">University studies: 1969–77</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: University studies: 1969–77">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>From 1969 to 1973, Bhutto studied for an undergraduate degree at <a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_College" title="Radcliffe College">Radcliffe College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia20085.E2.80.936Mu.C3.B1oz201349_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia20085.E2.80.936Mu.C3.B1oz201349-21">[21]</a></sup> Zulfikar asked his friend <a href="/wiki/John_Kenneth_Galbraith" title="John Kenneth Galbraith">John Kenneth Galbraith</a>, an economics professor at Harvard who had formerly been a U.S. ambassador to India, to be her local guardian.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200846_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200846-22">[22]</a></sup> Through him, Bhutto met his son <a href="/wiki/Peter_Galbraith" title="Peter Galbraith">Peter Galbraith</a>, who became a lifelong friend.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200846_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200846-22">[22]</a></sup> Murtaza joined Bhutto at Harvard a year later.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008106.E2.80.93107_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008106.E2.80.93107-23">[23]</a></sup> Bhutto later called her time at Harvard "four of the happiest years of my life".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201349_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201349-24">[24]</a></sup> She left Harvard with a <a href="/wiki/Bachelor_of_arts" class="mw-redirect" title="Bachelor of arts">bachelor of arts</a> degree with a major in governance.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201349_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201349-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Lady_Margaret_Hall_(6148510434).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Lady_Margaret_Hall_%286148510434%29.jpg/220px-Lady_Margaret_Hall_%286148510434%29.jpg" width="220" height="124" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Lady_Margaret_Hall_%286148510434%29.jpg/330px-Lady_Margaret_Hall_%286148510434%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Lady_Margaret_Hall_%286148510434%29.jpg/440px-Lady_Margaret_Hall_%286148510434%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2056" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Lady_Margaret_Hall_(6148510434).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
At the University of Oxford, Bhutto stayed at Lady Margaret Hall for her undergraduate studies</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>She then studied for a second undergraduate degree, in <a href="/wiki/Philosophy,_Politics_and_Economics" title="Philosophy, Politics and Economics">Philosophy, Politics and Economics</a>, at <a href="/wiki/Lady_Margaret_Hall" class="mw-redirect" title="Lady Margaret Hall">Lady Margaret Hall</a>, <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia20082.E2.80.933.2C_6_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia20082.E2.80.933.2C_6-25">[25]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> After LMH she attended <a href="/wiki/St_Catherine%27s_College,_Oxford" title="St Catherine's College, Oxford">St Catherine's College, Oxford</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> One of her fellow students at Oxford stated that there, she "epitomized the classic spoilt rich girl from a third world country".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia20083_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia20083-28">[28]</a></sup> She was elected President of the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Union" title="Oxford Union">Oxford Union</a> debating society,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200815_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200815-20">[20]</a></sup> the first Asian woman to hold that post.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201350_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201350-29">[29]</a></sup> She was also president of the Oxford Majlis Asian Society.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Despite the ongoing tensions between Pakistan and India, she interacted socially with Indian students,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia20084_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia20084-31">[31]</a></sup> and while at Oxford also made proposals of marriage to two fellow Pakistani students, but was rebuffed on both occasions.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia20084_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia20084-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>At Oxford, she led a campaign calling for the university to give her father an honorary degree; she gained the support of her father's old tutor, the historian <a href="/wiki/Hugh_Trevor_Roper" class="mw-redirect" title="Hugh Trevor Roper">Hugh Trevor Roper</a>. Bhutto's campaign was opposed by counter-protests, who believed that her father's supposed involvement in the persecution of <a href="/wiki/Sheikh_Mujibur_Rahman" title="Sheikh Mujibur Rahman">Sheikh Mujibur Rahman</a> and atrocities in Bangladesh made him unsuitable and ultimately the university declined to award the honorary degree.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia20087_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia20087-32">[32]</a></sup> In later years, Bhutto acknowledged that at this time she had been ignorant of many of the Pakistani Army's complicity in the atrocities in Bangladesh.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia20088_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia20088-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1971, while she was at Harvard, Zulfikar invited her to join him in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, where he was involved in a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a> meeting on the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Pakistani_War_of_1971" title="Indo-Pakistani War of 1971">Indo-Pakistani War</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200816_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200816-17">[17]</a></sup> In 1972, she accompanied her father to the India-Pakistan Summit in <a href="/wiki/Simla" class="mw-redirect" title="Simla">Simla</a> as a replacement for her mother, who was ill.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008xiii.E2.80.93xiv.2C_16Mu.C3.B1oz201344.E2.80.9345_34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008xiii.E2.80.93xiv.2C_16Mu.C3.B1oz201344.E2.80.9345-34">[34]</a></sup> There, she was introduced to the Indian Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Indira_Gandhi" title="Indira Gandhi">Indira Gandhi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200860_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200860-16">[16]</a></sup> While in Simla, she attracted much attention from both local and national Indian press.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200816_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200816-17">[17]</a></sup> In 1974, she was flown to <a href="/wiki/Lahore" title="Lahore">Lahore</a> to accompany her father at the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_Islamic_Cooperation" title="Organisation of Islamic Cooperation">Organisation of Islamic Cooperation</a>'s summit. Here, she met a number of senior Muslim world leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200816Mu.C3.B1oz201349_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200816Mu.C3.B1oz201349-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1976 she graduated from Oxford University, intent on joining the Pakistani Foreign Service.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200816_17-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200816-17">[17]</a></sup> Her father however insisted that she remain at Oxford for a further year to attain a postgraduate degree.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200816_17-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200816-17">[17]</a></sup> She returned to Pakistan in June 1977, where she was scheduled to work at the Prime Minister's office and the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Inter-Provincial_Council_of_Common_Interests&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Inter-Provincial Council of Common Interests (page does not exist)">Inter-Provincial Council of Common Interests</a> during the rest of the summer.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201350_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201350-29">[29]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Zia's_Pakistan"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Zia.27s_Pakistan">Zia's Pakistan</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Zia's Pakistan">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Zulfikar's_death_and_Benazir's_arrests:_1977–84"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Zulfikar.27s_death_and_Benazir.27s_arrests:_1977.E2.80.9384">Zulfikar's death and Benazir's arrests: 1977–84</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Zulfikar's death and Benazir's arrests: 1977–84">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1977, Zulfikar Bhutto was overthrown in a military coup led by General <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Zia-ul-Haq" title="Muhammad Zia-ul-Haq">Muhammad Zia-ul-Haq</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Army_Staff_(Pakistan)" title="Chief of Army Staff (Pakistan)">Chief of Army Staff</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013274_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013274-4">[4]</a></sup> In July, Zia declared Pakistan to be under <a href="/wiki/Martial_law" title="Martial law">martial law</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013274_4-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013274-4">[4]</a></sup> Zulfikar was then arrested and charged with the murder of Muhammad Ahmed Khan Kauri.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013274_4-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013274-4">[4]</a></sup> After the coup, Bhutto's brothers were sent abroad to canvass international support for their father.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200818Mu.C3.B1oz201351_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200818Mu.C3.B1oz201351-36">[36]</a></sup> Bhutto and her mother remained in Pakistan, although were repeatedly detained for short periods.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200818_37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200818-37">[37]</a></sup> When she was able, Bhutto visited her father in prison.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200818_37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200818-37">[37]</a></sup> She also assisted in the preparation of his defence case, which was put before first the <a href="/wiki/Lahore_High_Court" title="Lahore High Court">Lahore High Court</a>, which sentenced him to death, and then the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Court_of_Pakistan" title="Supreme Court of Pakistan">Supreme Court</a>, which upheld that decision.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200819_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200819-38">[38]</a></sup> Just before his execution, Zulfikar urged his wife and daughter to leave Pakistan, but they refused.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200819_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200819-38">[38]</a></sup> He was executed in April 1979.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013274Mu.C3.B1oz201347_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013274Mu.C3.B1oz201347-39">[39]</a></sup> Bhutto and Nusrat were then imprisoned for six months, before being released and placed under <a href="/wiki/House_arrest" title="House arrest">house arrest</a> for a further six months. The pair were only fully released in April 1980.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200847_40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200847-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>Benazir and her mother were appointed co-chairpersons of the PPP.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013274_4-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013274-4">[4]</a></sup> In February 1981 she formally established the <a href="/wiki/Movement_for_the_Restoration_of_Democracy" title="Movement for the Restoration of Democracy">Movement for the Restoration of Democracy</a> (MRD), a group that brought together the PPP with other political parties in the country: the <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Muslim_League" title="Pakistan Muslim League">Pakistan Muslim League</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Democratic_Party" title="Pakistan Democratic Party">Pakistan Democratic Party</a>, Pakistan MazdoorKisan Party, Pakistan MazdoorKisan Party, <a href="/wiki/National_Awami_Party_(Wali)" title="National Awami Party (Wali)">National Awami Party</a>, QuomiMahaz-e-Azadi, Jamiat-i-Ulema-i-Islam, and the Tahrik-i-Istiqlal.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013274.E2.80.93275_41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013274.E2.80.93275-41">[41]</a></sup> The MRD called for a four-point programme: an end to martial law, the restoration of the 1973 constitution, parliamentary elections, and the transfer of political power from the military to the elected representatives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013275_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013275-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Her brothers, Murtaza and Shahnawaz, turned to paramilitary action, founding the <a href="/wiki/Al_Zulfikar" class="mw-redirect" title="Al Zulfikar">Al Zulfikar</a> group which trained its members to carry out acts of assassination and sabotage to oust the military government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200820_43-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200820-43">[43]</a></sup> After Al Zulfikar orchestrated the <a href="/wiki/1981_Pakistan_International_Airlines_hijacking" title="1981 Pakistan International Airlines hijacking">1981 Pakistan International Airlines hijacking</a>, the government used this as the pretext for re-arresting Bhutto and her mother in March.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200847KhuhroSoomro2013275_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200847KhuhroSoomro2013275-44">[44]</a></sup> Bhutto disapproved of the hijacking, believing that it strengthened Zia's hand.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201363_45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201363-45">[45]</a></sup> In July 1981, Nusrat was released on the grounds of ill health, but Bhutto was not.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200847_40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200847-40">[40]</a></sup> She was kept for a time in Karachi before being moved to <a href="/wiki/Sukkur" title="Sukkur">Sukkur</a> prison and then back again to Karachi.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200847_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200847-40">[40]</a></sup> During much of this period, she was held in <a href="/wiki/Solitary_confinement" title="Solitary confinement">solitary confinement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200847.E2.80.9348_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200847.E2.80.9348-46">[46]</a></sup> In the United States—a key ally of Zia's regime—Peter Galbraith helped rally support for Bhutto, most notably from the politicians <a href="/wiki/Claiborne_Pell" title="Claiborne Pell">Claiborne Pell</a> and <a href="/wiki/James_L._Buckley" title="James L. Buckley">James Buckley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200848.E2.80.9349_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200848.E2.80.9349-47">[47]</a></sup> When Zia visited Washington D.C. in December 1982, they raised the issue of Bhutto's imprisonment with him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200849.E2.80.9350_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200849.E2.80.9350-48">[48]</a></sup> As international pressure mounted, the Pakistani government agreed to release her,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013275_42-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013275-42">[42]</a></sup> putting her on a flight to Geneva in January 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200852_49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200852-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Release_and_self-imposed_exile:_1984–87"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Release_and_self-imposed_exile:_1984.E2.80.9387">Release and self-imposed exile: 1984–87</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Release and self-imposed exile: 1984–87">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Barbicanestatefromabove.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Barbicanestatefromabove.jpg/220px-Barbicanestatefromabove.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Barbicanestatefromabove.jpg/330px-Barbicanestatefromabove.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Barbicanestatefromabove.jpg/440px-Barbicanestatefromabove.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Barbicanestatefromabove.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
While in exile, Bhutto lived in central London's Barbican Estate</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>From Geneva she proceeded to the United Kingdom, renting a flat in London's <a href="/wiki/Barbican_Estate" title="Barbican Estate">Barbican Estate</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199114Bhatia2008107_50-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199114Bhatia2008107-50">[50]</a></sup> In March she made a visit to <a href="/wiki/Washington_D.C." class="mw-redirect" title="Washington D.C.">Washington D.C.</a>, where she met with middle-ranking government officials but was kept at bay by the governing administration of President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200852_49-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200852-49">[49]</a></sup> Bhutto made several additional visits to the United States over the coming few years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200853_51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200853-51">[51]</a></sup> While in exile, Benazir became a rallying point for the PPP.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200820_43-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200820-43">[43]</a></sup> Murtaza believed that it was he, and not Benazir, who was their father's designated political heir; as evidence, he cited that he had been asked to manage his father's Larkana constituency in the 1977 general election.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008xiii_52-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008xiii-52">[52]</a></sup> Bhutto biographer Shyam Bhatia thought that this was probably Zulfikar's intention, as the latter would have recognised the significant impediments to a woman being elected leader in a conservative Islamic society like Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200819_38-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200819-38">[38]</a></sup> Benazir nevertheless maintained that her father had always wanted her to become a politician.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008xiii_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008xiii-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>In July 1985, Shahnawaz died under unexplained circumstances in the French city of <a href="/wiki/Cannes" title="Cannes">Cannes</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200821Mu.C3.B1oz201358_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200821Mu.C3.B1oz201358-53">[53]</a></sup> Bhutto believed that Shahnawaz's murder had been directly ordered by Zia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200810_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200810-54">[54]</a></sup> Zia's government allowed her to bring her brother's body to Pakistan in August, where it could be interned in the family cemetery at Larkana.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200821Mu.C3.B1oz201359_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200821Mu.C3.B1oz201359-55">[55]</a></sup> Shortly after the burial, she was arrested and detained under house arrest until November, at which point she was released and returned to Europe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200822Mu.C3.B1oz201359_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200822Mu.C3.B1oz201359-56">[56]</a></sup> In December 1985, martial law was lifted in Pakistan and Bhutto decided to return home. She arrived at <a href="/wiki/Lahore_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Lahore Airport">Lahore Airport</a> in April 1986, where she was greeted by a large crowd.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200820.E2.80.9321KhuhroSoomro2013275_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200820.E2.80.9321KhuhroSoomro2013275-57">[57]</a></sup> An estimated two million people came to see her speak at <a href="/wiki/Iqbal_Park" title="Iqbal Park">Iqbal Park</a>, where she rallied against Zia's regime.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200821_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200821-58">[58]</a></sup> She then visited both Sindh and <a href="/wiki/Punjab,_Pakistan" title="Punjab, Pakistan">Punjab</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200822_59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200822-59">[59]</a></sup> In August, she was again arrested and briefly detained for several weeks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200822KhuhroSoomro2013275_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200822KhuhroSoomro2013275-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>Back in Pakistan, she agreed to an <a href="/wiki/Arranged_marriage" title="Arranged marriage">arranged marriage</a>; her mother selected <a href="/wiki/Asif_Ali_Zardari" title="Asif Ali Zardari">Asif Ali Zardari</a> as a suitable husband.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200828.2C_29_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200828.2C_29-61">[61]</a></sup> The wedding took place in Karachi in December 1987,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb19916Mu.C3.B1oz201356_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb19916Mu.C3.B1oz201356-62">[62]</a></sup> and was billed as the "People's Wedding", serving as a <i>de facto</i> political rally.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb19915.E2.80.936_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb19915.E2.80.936-63">[63]</a></sup> Bhutto would have been aware that being married gave her an image of respectability which would improve her chances of being elected.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199188Mu.C3.B1oz201356_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199188Mu.C3.B1oz201356-64">[64]</a></sup> After the wedding, she soon became pregnant with her son, <a href="/wiki/Bilawal_Bhutto_Zardari" title="Bilawal Bhutto Zardari">Bilawal</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200830_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200830-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Electoral_campaign:_1988">Electoral campaign: 1988</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Electoral campaign: 1988">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In May 1988, Zia dissolved the assemblies and called a November election.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200822KhuhroSoomro2013275_60-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200822KhuhroSoomro2013275-60">[60]</a></sup> He had not wanted Bhutto to win, and his choice of date may have been deliberately chosen to coincide with the date when Bhutto was projected to give birth, thus hindering her ability to campaign.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200853KhuhroSoomro2013275_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200853KhuhroSoomro2013275-66">[66]</a></sup> He also sought to hinder her chances by declaring that the election would be held on a non-party basis, with candidates standing as individuals rather than as representatives of a political party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013275Mu.C3.B1oz201353_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013275Mu.C3.B1oz201353-67">[67]</a></sup> Bhutto and the PPP launched a legal challenge against this latter stipulation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013275Mu.C3.B1oz201353_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKhuhroSoomro2013275Mu.C3.B1oz201353-67">[67]</a></sup> In August, Zia suddenly died when <a href="/wiki/Death_and_state_funeral_of_Muhammad_Zia-ul-Haq" title="Death and state funeral of Muhammad Zia-ul-Haq">his aircraft crashed</a> shortly after take-off from <a href="/wiki/Bahawalpur_Airport" title="Bahawalpur Airport">Bahawalpur Airport</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199188.E2.80.9389Bhatia200822KhuhroSoomro2013275Mu.C3.B1oz201353.E2.80.9354_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199188.E2.80.9389Bhatia200822KhuhroSoomro2013275Mu.C3.B1oz201353.E2.80.9354-68">[68]</a></sup> A joint U.S.-Pakistani investigation was unable to ascertain the cause of the crash, although sabotage was widely suspected.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201354.E2.80.9355_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201354.E2.80.9355-69">[69]</a></sup> After his death, the Supreme Court announced that the election should take place on a party basis, rather than the non-party basis that Zia had desired.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199145Bhatia200891KhuhroSoomro2013275_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199145Bhatia200891KhuhroSoomro2013275-70">[70]</a></sup> Bhutto insisted that the PPP campaign separately from the MRD,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199153_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199153-71">[71]</a></sup> and dropped its socialist platform in favour of economic <a href="/wiki/Thatcherism" title="Thatcherism">Thatcherism</a> and a commitment to the <a href="/wiki/Free_market" title="Free market">free market</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199156_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199156-72">[72]</a></sup> Amid predictions that the PPP would win, it received 18,000 prospective candidates, many offering the party money for their selection; this influx of new members and candidates caused upset among many established members, who felt that Bhutto was deserting them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199154.E2.80.9355_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199154.E2.80.9355-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Pakistan_People%27s_Part.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Flag_of_Pakistan_People%27s_Part.svg/220px-Flag_of_Pakistan_People%27s_Part.svg.png" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Flag_of_Pakistan_People%27s_Part.svg/330px-Flag_of_Pakistan_People%27s_Part.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Flag_of_Pakistan_People%27s_Part.svg/440px-Flag_of_Pakistan_People%27s_Part.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Pakistan_People%27s_Part.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The flag of the Pakistan People's Party, which Bhutto co-chaired after her father's execution</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Islamic_fundamentalism" title="Islamic fundamentalism">Islamic fundamentalists</a> spoke out against the idea of a woman leader, claiming that it was un-Islamic.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199139_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199139-74">[74]</a></sup> Their propaganda foregrounded what they presented as her un-Islamic behaviour, including a photo of her dancing in a Parisian nightclub.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199139_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199139-74">[74]</a></sup> Zia loyalists and Islamic fundamentalists united to form a new political party, <a href="/wiki/Islami_Jamhoori_Ittehad" title="Islami Jamhoori Ittehad">Islami Jamhoori Ittehad</a> (IJI),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199158.E2.80.9359KhuhroSoomro2013275_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199158.E2.80.9359KhuhroSoomro2013275-75">[75]</a></sup> which was funded by the <a href="/wiki/Inter-Services_Intelligence" title="Inter-Services Intelligence">Inter-Services Intelligence</a> (ISI).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200891_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200891-76">[76]</a></sup> The ISI also engaged in vote rigging in an attempt to secure an IJI victory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200891Mu.C3.B1oz201356_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200891Mu.C3.B1oz201356-77">[77]</a></sup> Despite these attempts, Bhutto led the PPP to victory in the election, taking 93 of the 205 contested seats.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199145Bhatia200822.2C_91KhuhroSoomro2013275_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199145Bhatia200822.2C_91KhuhroSoomro2013275-78">[78]</a></sup> The IJI took only 54 seats.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200891_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200891-76">[76]</a></sup> This meant that the PPP had the largest number of seats, although not a clear majority.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199116.2C_45Mu.C3.B1oz201359_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199116.2C_45Mu.C3.B1oz201359-79">[79]</a></sup> Both the PPP and IJI courted independent MPs hoping to woo them to their side,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199149.E2.80.9350_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199149.E2.80.9350-80">[80]</a></sup> and unsuccessful attempts were also made by the country's right-wing forces to convince some of the elected PPP parliamentarians to switch allegiance to the IJI.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200891_76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200891-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<p>Two weeks later, President <a href="/wiki/Ghulam_Ishaq_Khan" title="Ghulam Ishaq Khan">Ghulam Ishaq Khan</a> reluctantly invited Bhutto to form the next government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200855.2C_92_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200855.2C_92-81">[81]</a></sup> To do so, she formed a <a href="/wiki/Coalition_government" title="Coalition government">coalition</a> with the <a href="/wiki/Muttahida_Qaumi_Movement" title="Muttahida Qaumi Movement">Muttahida Qaumi Movement</a> (MQM) party, which had 13 seats in parliament,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199146_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199146-82">[82]</a></sup> an action that upset the <a href="/wiki/Sindhi_nationalism" title="Sindhi nationalism">Sindhi nationalist</a> faction within her party.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199148_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199148-83">[83]</a></sup> She was sworn in as the Prime Minister of Pakistan in December.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200822Akhter200968_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200822Akhter200968-84">[84]</a></sup> Bhutto became the world's fourth ever woman Prime Minister and the first in a Muslim-majority country,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199116Bhatia200822_85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199116Bhatia200822-85">[85]</a></sup> the youngest elected leader in the Islamic world,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201359_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201359-86">[86]</a></sup> the world's youngest Prime Minister,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199130_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199130-87">[87]</a></sup> and Pakistan's second nationally elected Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199121_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199121-88">[88]</a></sup> After her election, party workers were encouraged to refer to her as "Mohtarma".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia20081_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia20081-89">[89]</a></sup> There was hope among many observers that her premiership would mark a new era of multi-party democracy, growing gender equality, and better relations with India.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETalbot2009287_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETalbot2009287-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="First_term_as_Prime_Minister_(1988–90)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_term_as_Prime_Minister_.281988.E2.80.9390.29">First term as Prime Minister (1988–90)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: First term as Prime Minister (1988–90)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Benazir_bhutto_1988.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Benazir_bhutto_1988.jpg/220px-Benazir_bhutto_1988.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Benazir_bhutto_1988.jpg/330px-Benazir_bhutto_1988.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Benazir_bhutto_1988.jpg/440px-Benazir_bhutto_1988.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2830" data-file-height="1900" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Benazir_bhutto_1988.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Benazir Bhutto on a visit to Washington, D.C. in 1989. As a politician she took to wearing the white <i><a href="/wiki/Dupatta" title="Dupatta">dupatta</a></i> on her head, a political move to attract support from Islamic clerics.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEM.C5.A9noz201360_91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEM.C5.A9noz201360-91">[91]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Bhutto created the largest cabinet in Pakistan's history,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199172_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199172-92">[92]</a></sup> although her administration consisted of figures with little political experience.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199147_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199147-93">[93]</a></sup> Following her election, there remained significant mistrust between Bhutto and the right-wing military administration; many senior military figures viewed her, like her father, as a threat to their dominant role in Pakistan's political arena.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200880_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200880-94">[94]</a></sup> The country's three most powerful figures—the army chief <a href="/wiki/Aslam_Beg" class="mw-redirect" title="Aslam Beg">Aslam Beg</a>, the ISI chief <a href="/wiki/Hamid_Gul" title="Hamid Gul">Hamid Gul</a>, and President Khan—all had contempt for her family and for the idea that a woman was Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200889.E2.80.9390_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200889.E2.80.9390-95">[95]</a></sup> This opposition was substantial and contributed to Bhutto's inability to pass any major legislation during her first term in office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb1991111Bhatia200889_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb1991111Bhatia200889-96">[96]</a></sup> She was nevertheless able to ensure the release of a number of <a href="/wiki/Political_prisoner" title="Political prisoner">political prisoners</a> detained under the Zia government,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb1991111Bhatia200898_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb1991111Bhatia200898-97">[97]</a></sup> as well as lifting the country's bans on trade unions and student associations,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEM.C5.A9noz201360_91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEM.C5.A9noz201360-91">[91]</a></sup> and removing many of the constraints imposed on <a href="/wiki/Non-governmental_organisations" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-governmental organisations">non-governmental organisations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEM.C5.A9noz201360_91-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEM.C5.A9noz201360-91">[91]</a></sup> She also introduced measures to lift the <a href="/wiki/Media_censorship" class="mw-redirect" title="Media censorship">media censorship</a> introduced by previous military administrations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200898_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200898-98">[98]</a></sup> She entrusted <a href="/w/index.php?title=Shamsul_Hasan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Shamsul Hasan (page does not exist)">Shamsul Hasan</a> with dismantling the <a href="/w/index.php?title=National_Press_Trust&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="National Press Trust (page does not exist)">National Press Trust</a>, a conglomerate of over 15 newspapers, but President Khan delayed signing the documents and thus the Trust would only be broken up during her second premiership.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200898_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200898-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bhutto succeeded in getting Khan's approval to change two of the country's four provincial governors;<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200894_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200894-99">[99]</a></sup> she appointed General <a href="/wiki/Tikka_Khan" title="Tikka Khan">Tikka Khan</a>, one of the few senior military officers who was loyal to her, as the Governor of Punjab.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200881_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200881-100">[100]</a></sup> She also sought to replace the chair of the joint chiefs of staff, <a href="/wiki/Iftikhar_Ahmed_Sirohey" title="Iftikhar Ahmed Sirohey">Iftikhar Ahmed Sirohey</a>, but President Khan refused to permit this.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200894_99-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200894-99">[99]</a></sup> Bhutto disliked Khan's hostile attitude toward her, but he had the backing of the military.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200893_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200893-101">[101]</a></sup> In the presidential election, Bhutto initially proposed <a href="/w/index.php?title=Malik_Qasim&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Malik Qasim (page does not exist)">Malik Qasim</a>, who had been involved in the MRD, as the PPP's nominee, but the military refused to accept this. Bhutto relented and agreed that Khan could be nominated as the PPP's presidential candidate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200893.E2.80.9394_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200893.E2.80.9394-102">[102]</a></sup> Bhutto also wanted to replace <a href="/wiki/Mahbub_ul_Haq" title="Mahbub ul Haq">Mahbub ul Haq</a> as finance minister, but again the military opposed her. Compromising, she accepted al Huq's continued role as finance minister but appointed <a href="/wiki/Wasim_Jafri" title="Wasim Jafri">Wasim Jafri</a> as her financial advisor.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200893_101-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200893-101">[101]</a></sup> Beg made it clear to Bhutto that the military would not tolerate her interference in their control of defence and foreign affairs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200893_101-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200893-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>Among the problems facing Pakistan when Bhutto took the Premiership was soaring employment and high unemployment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200899_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200899-103">[103]</a></sup> The Pakistani government was bankrupt, with Zia having borrowed at high interest rates to pay government wages.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199147_93-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199147-93">[93]</a></sup> The country also faced a growing problem with the illegal narcotics trade, with Pakistan being one of the world's highest <a href="/wiki/Heroin" title="Heroin">heroin</a> exporters and having rapidly growing levels of domestic usage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200899_103-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200899-103">[103]</a></sup> Bhutto promised that she would take tough action on the powerful drug barons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199147_93-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199147-93">[93]</a></sup> 60% of the country's population lived in Punjab province, which was under the control of Zia's protégé, <a href="/wiki/Nawaz_Sharif" title="Nawaz Sharif">Nawaz Sharif</a>, as provincial Chief Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199148_83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199148-83">[83]</a></sup> Both Sharif and Bhutto attempted to remove the other from power,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199198.E2.80.9399_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199198.E2.80.9399-104">[104]</a></sup> with Bhutto accusing Sharif of having rigged the election to become Chief Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199198_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199198-105">[105]</a></sup> Sharif benefited from growing Punjabi chauvinism toward the country's Sindhi minority,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199199_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199199-106">[106]</a></sup> as well as a perception that Bhutto—a Sindhi—was attacking the Punjab.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb1991100_107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb1991100-107">[107]</a></sup> Although Bhutto had long supported greater autonomy for Pakistan's provinces, she opposed it in the case of the Punjab.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb1991112.E2.80.93113_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb1991112.E2.80.93113-108">[108]</a></sup> Sharif's Punjabi authority refused to accept the federal officials whom Bhutto posted there.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb1991112_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb1991112-109">[109]</a></sup> Relations between Bhutto and Pakistan's civil service also deteriorated, causing paralysis of many state affairs; Bhutto spoke of it as "Zia's bureaucracy" and her perceived anti-Punjabi stance impacted many civil servants, of whom 80% were Punjabi.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb1991100.2C_107.E2.80.93109_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb1991100.2C_107.E2.80.93109-110">[110]</a></sup> In April 1989, opposition parties organised a parliamentary no-confidence vote in Bhutto's leadership, but it was defeated by 12 votes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAkhter200978Mu.C3.B1oz201360.E2.80.9361_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAkhter200978Mu.C3.B1oz201360.E2.80.9361-111">[111]</a></sup> Bhutto claimed that many National Assembly voters had been bribed to vote against her, with $10 million having been supplied for this by a Saudi Salafi cleric, <a href="/wiki/Osama_bin_Laden" title="Osama bin Laden">Osama bin Laden</a>, who sought to overthrow her government and replace it with an Islamic theocracy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201360.E2.80.9361_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201360.E2.80.9361-112">[112]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy">Foreign policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Foreign policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During her first premiership, Bhutto went on a number of foreign trips, enhancing her image as the first female Prime Minister in the Islamic world.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200898_98-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200898-98">[98]</a></sup> In 1989, she attended the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Heads_of_Government_Meeting_1989" title="Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting 1989">Commonwealth Heads of Government Meeting</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kuala_Lumpur" title="Kuala Lumpur">Kuala Lumpur</a>, where Pakistan was re-admitted to the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_of_Nations" title="Commonwealth of Nations">Commonwealth of Nations</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200898_98-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200898-98">[98]</a></sup> Bhutto attempted to improve relations with neighbouring India, harking back to the Simla Accords.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAkhter200975M.C5.A9noz201360_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAkhter200975M.C5.A9noz201360-113">[113]</a></sup> Bhutto claimed that she terminated support for <a href="/wiki/Khalistan_movement" title="Khalistan movement">Sikh separatists</a> active in India, something which Zia had encouraged to destabilise Indian control in the Punjab.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200861_114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200861-114">[114]</a></sup> After being accused of being soft on India, she took a harder line against Pakistan's neighbour, particularly on the <a href="/wiki/Kashmir_conflict" title="Kashmir conflict">Kashmir conflict</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb1991111_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb1991111-115">[115]</a></sup> In 1990, she made a speech in which she incited Kashmiri Muslims to rise up against their administration,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200861_114-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200861-114">[114]</a></sup> and in interviews expressed support for the Kashmiri Muslim community.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200862_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200862-116">[116]</a></sup> In 1990, Major General <a href="/wiki/Pervez_Musharraf" title="Pervez Musharraf">Pervez Musharraf</a> proposed a military invasion of <a href="/wiki/Kargil" title="Kargil">Kargil</a> as part of an attempt to annex Kashmir; Bhutto refused to back the plan, believing that the international condemnation would be severe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200885_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200885-117">[117]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-kapur_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kapur-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p>As Premier, Bhutto was reluctant to challenge the ISI's support for the Islamist <a href="/wiki/Mujahideen" title="Mujahideen">mujahideen</a> forces in Afghanistan which were then <a href="/wiki/Civil_war_in_Afghanistan_(1989%E2%80%931992)" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil war in Afghanistan (1989–1992)">engaged in a civil war</a> against the <a href="/wiki/Democratic_Republic_of_Afghanistan" title="Democratic Republic of Afghanistan">country's Marxist–Leninist government</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200890_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200890-119">[119]</a></sup> In April 1989, Gul led an invasion of Afghanistan with the purpose of seizing <a href="/wiki/Jalalabad" title="Jalalabad">Jalalabad</a>, which was then to be formed into a capital from which the country's anti-Soviet, Islamist-dominated opposition forces could operate.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200892.E2.80.9393_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200892.E2.80.9393-120">[120]</a></sup> When the operation failed and the Pakistanis were driven out by the Afghan Army, Gul blamed Bhutto's administration for the failure, claiming that someone in her entourage had leaked details of the mission of the Afghan government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200895_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200895-121">[121]</a></sup> Gul was too powerful for Bhutto to force him into retirement, but she did transfer him from the ISI to another section of the military.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201360_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201360-122">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Science_policy">Science policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Science policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Grjoshi.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Grjoshi.jpg/220px-Grjoshi.jpg" width="220" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Grjoshi.jpg/330px-Grjoshi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/Grjoshi.jpg/440px-Grjoshi.jpg 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="413" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Grjoshi.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Benazir at an award ceremony in 1990</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>After Bhutto became Prime Minister, President Khan and the military were reluctant to tell her about Pakistan's nuclear programme.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200876_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200876-123">[123]</a></sup> On a trip to the US, she told Congress that "we do not possess, nor do we intend to make, a nuclear device".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEM.C5.A9noz2013100_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEM.C5.A9noz2013100-124">[124]</a></sup> Between January and March 1989, she authorised cold tests of nuclear weaponry, without fissionable material.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200876_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200876-123">[123]</a></sup></p>
<p>During her 1990 trip to Britain, Bhutto paid a visit to Dr. <a href="/wiki/Abdus_Salam" title="Abdus Salam">Abdus Salam</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Nobel_laureates_in_physics" class="mw-redirect" title="Nobel laureates in physics">Nobel laureate in physics</a> and a science advisor to her father's government. During both her terms as Prime Minister, Bhutto followed the science and technology policy her father laid out in 1972, and promoted <a href="/wiki/Military_funding_of_science" class="mw-redirect" title="Military funding of science">military funding of science and technology</a> as part of that policy. However, in 1988, Bhutto was denied access to the classified national research institutes run by the military, which remained however under the control of the civilian president <a href="/wiki/Ghulam_Ishaq_Khan" title="Ghulam Ishaq Khan">Ghulam Ishaq Khan</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Army_Staff" title="Chief of Army Staff">Chief of Army Staff</a>. Bhutto was kept unaware about the progress of the nuclear complexes, even when the country passed the milestone in 1986<sup id="cite_ref-125" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> of fissile core manufacturing capability U.S. Ambassador Robert Oakley was the first diplomat notified about the complexes, in 1988.<sup id="cite_ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Shortly afterwards Bhutto summoned chairman of the <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Atomic_Energy_Commission" title="Pakistan Atomic Energy Commission">Pakistan Atomic Energy Commission</a>, <a href="/wiki/Munir_Ahmad_Khan" title="Munir Ahmad Khan">Munir Ahmad Khan</a> to her office; Khan brought <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Qadeer_Khan" title="Abdul Qadeer Khan">Abdul Qadeer Khan</a> with him and introduced him to the Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-DavidIsenber_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DavidIsenber-127">[127]</a></sup></p>
<p>At that meeting Bhutto learned the status of this program which had matured since its beginnings in 1978, and on request of A. Q. Khan, visited <a href="/wiki/Khan_Research_Laboratories" title="Khan Research Laboratories">Khan Research Laboratories</a> for the first time in 1989, much to the anger of Ishaq Khan.<sup id="cite_ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-LongRoad_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LongRoad-129">[129]</a></sup> Bhutto also responded to Khan<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (May 2016)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> when she moved the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Science_and_Technology_(Pakistan)" title="Ministry of Science and Technology (Pakistan)">Ministry of Science and Technology</a>'s office to the Prime Minister Secretariat with Munir Ahmad Khan directly reporting to her.<sup id="cite_ref-LongRoad_129-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LongRoad-129">[129]</a></sup> Bhutto had successfully eliminated any possibility of Khan's involvement and prevented him from having any influence in science-research programmes, a policy which also benefited her successor <a href="/wiki/Nawaz_Sharif" title="Nawaz Sharif">Nawaz Sharif</a>. During both her prime-ministerial terms Bhutto funded many projects entirely devoted to the country's national defence and security. The dismissal of Lieutenant-General Gul by Benazir Bhutto had played a significant role on Chief of Army Staff General <a href="/wiki/Mirza_Aslam_Beg" title="Mirza Aslam Beg">Mirza Aslam Beg</a>, who did not interfere in matters pertaining to science and technology, and remained supportive towards Benazir Bhutto's hard-line actions against the President.<sup id="cite_ref-LongRoad_129-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LongRoad-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<table class="rquote floatleft" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:left; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">
<tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">“</td>
<td style="padding:0 10px;">If we don't, India will go ahead and adopt aggressive designs on us... To preserve the <a href="/wiki/Minimum_deterrence" class="mw-redirect" title="Minimum deterrence">minimum deterrence</a>, tests should be performed this month of year....</td>
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:1em; text-align:right;"><cite>—&#160;Benazir Bhutto, 1998, <sup id="cite_ref-130" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup></cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>In the 1980s, Benazir Bhutto started aerospace projects such as <i><a href="/wiki/Project_Sabre_II" title="Project Sabre II">Project Sabre II</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/CAC/PAC_JF-17_Thunder#Background" title="CAC/PAC JF-17 Thunder">Project PAC</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ghauri_(missile)" title="Ghauri (missile)">Ghauri project</a></i> under Dr. Abdul Qadeer Khan in 1990 and the <i><a href="/wiki/Shaheen-I" title="Shaheen-I">Shaheen programme</a></i> in 1995 under Dr. Samar Mubarakmand.<sup id="cite_ref-19980516Dawn_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19980516Dawn-131">[131]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Saheen_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saheen-132">[132]</a></sup></p>
<p>In opposition to her conservative opponent Nawaz Sharif, whose policy was to make the nuclear weapons programme benefit the <a href="/wiki/Economy_of_Pakistan" title="Economy of Pakistan">economy</a>, Benazir Bhutto took aggressive steps to modernise and expand the integrated atomic weapons programme begun by her father in 1972, who was one of the key political administrative figures of Pakistan's <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_and_its_Nuclear_Deterrent_Program" class="mw-redirect" title="Pakistan and its Nuclear Deterrent Program">nuclear deterrent development</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Hurry_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hurry-133">[133]</a></sup> During her first term, Benazir Bhutto established the separate but integrated nuclear testing programme in the atomic bomb programme, requiring the authorisation of the Prime minister and the military leadership.<sup id="cite_ref-Atta-oul-Kasmie_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Atta-oul-Kasmie-134">[134]</a></sup> Despite Benazir's denial that she authorised the nuclear testing programme in her second term she continued to modernise the programme which she termed a "contractual obligation".<sup id="cite_ref-Dawn1_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dawn1-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<table class="rquote floatright" role="presentation" style="border-collapse:collapse; border-style:none; float:right; margin:0.5em 0.75em; width:33%;">
<tr style="text-align:left; vertical-align:top;">
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">“</td>
<td style="padding:0 10px;">It took only two weeks and three days for Pakistan to master the [atomic] field... and (detonate) the nuclear devices of our own...</td>
<td style="color:#b2b7f2; font-size:3.3em; font-family:'Times New Roman',serif; font-weight:bold; padding:4px 2px 2px; text-align:right; vertical-align:bottom; width:0.5em; line-height: 1em;">”</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td class="rquotecite" colspan="3" style="padding-top:10px; font-size:smaller; line-height:1em; text-align:right;"><cite>—&#160;Benazir Bhutto, on first nuclear tests on May 1998<sup id="cite_ref-Broad_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Broad-136">[136]</a></sup></cite></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>It was during her régime that the <a href="/wiki/Pressler_amendment" class="mw-redirect" title="Pressler amendment">Pressler amendment</a> came into effect, an attempt to freeze the programme.<sup id="cite_ref-Dawn1_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dawn1-135">[135]</a></sup> During frequent trips to the United States, Bhutto refused to compromise on the nuclear weapons programme, and attacked the Indian nuclear programme on multiple occasions.<sup id="cite_ref-Dawn1_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dawn1-135">[135]</a></sup> Benazir Bhutto misled the U.S. when she told them that the programme had been frozen; the programme was progressively modernized and continued under her watch.<sup id="cite_ref-Hurry_133-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hurry-133">[133]</a></sup> Under her regime, the <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Atomic_Energy_Commission" title="Pakistan Atomic Energy Commission">Pakistan Atomic Energy Commission</a> (PAEC) conducted series of improvised designs of nuclear weapons designed by the Theoretical Physics Group (TPG) at PAEC.<sup id="cite_ref-Hurry_133-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hurry-133">[133]</a></sup> Benazir Bhutto also carried messages to <a href="/wiki/Munir_Ahmad_Khan" title="Munir Ahmad Khan">Munir Ahmad Khan</a> from her father and back in 1979 as her father, Zulfikar Ali Bhutto, had instructed his daughter to remain in touch with the Chairman of PAEC.<sup id="cite_ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137">[nb 1]</a></sup> In this context, Bhutto had appointed Munir Ahmad Khan as her <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Science_and_Technology_(Pakistan)#Science_Advisors" title="Ministry of Science and Technology (Pakistan)">Science Adviser</a>, and he kept her informed about the development of the programme. In all, the nuclear weapons and energy program remained a top priority, along with the country's economy. During her first term, the nuclear program was under attack and under pressure from the Western world, particularly the United States. Despite economic aid offered by the European Union and the United States in return for halting or freezing the program, Benazir continued the program in both her first and second terms.<sup id="cite_ref-Atta-oul-Kasmie_134-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Atta-oul-Kasmie-134">[134]</a></sup></p>
<p>During her first term, Bhutto approved and launched the <a href="/wiki/Shaheen-I" title="Shaheen-I">Shaheen programme</a> and advocated for the programme. Bhutto also allotted funds for the programme.<sup id="cite_ref-Atta-oul-Kasmie_134-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Atta-oul-Kasmie-134">[134]</a></sup> On 6 January 1996, Bhutto publicly announced that if India conducted a nuclear test, Pakistan could be forced to "follow suit".<sup id="cite_ref-138" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-138">[137]</a></sup> Bhutto later said that the day will never arise when we have to use our knowledge to make and detonate a [nuclear] device and export our technology.<sup id="cite_ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139">[138]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The People of (Pakistan) ... are "security conscious" because of the (1971) severe trauma, and the three wars with (India). Our (Pakistan) nuclear development was peaceful ... but was "an effective deterrence to India" ... because (New Delhi) had detonated a nuclear device. She (Pakistan) ..., thus, had to take every step to ensure its territorial integrity and sovereignty...</p>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>Benazir Bhutto, <small>on Pakistan's nuclear weapons</small>, <sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[139]</a></sup></cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1989_military_scandal">1989 military scandal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 1989 military scandal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1989, the media reported a sting operation and political scandal, codenamed <a href="/wiki/Operation_Midnight_Jackal" title="Operation Midnight Jackal">Midnight Jackal</a>, in which former members of ISI hatched a plan to topple the Bhutto government. Midnight Jackal was a political intelligence operation launched under President Ghulam Ishaq Khan and army chief General Mirza Aslam Beg, whose objective was to pass a <a href="/wiki/No-confidence_motion" class="mw-redirect" title="No-confidence motion">no-confidence motion</a> in Parliament by bribing and threatening parliament members. Lieutenant-General <a href="/wiki/Asif_Nawaz" title="Asif Nawaz">Asif Nawaz</a> had suspected the activities of Brigadier-General <a href="/wiki/Imtiaz_Ahmed_(brigadier)" title="Imtiaz Ahmed (brigadier)">Imtiaz Ahmed</a>, therefore, a watch cell unit was dispatched to keep an eye on him.<sup id="cite_ref-20090828DailyTimes_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20090828DailyTimes-141">[140]</a></sup></p>
<p>This operation was exposed by ISI when it obtained a VHS tape containing the conversation between two former army officers and former members of ISI, from the <a href="/wiki/Intelligence_Bureau_(Pakistan)" title="Intelligence Bureau (Pakistan)">Intelligence Bureau</a> (IB). The tape was confiscated by ISI director-general Lieutenant-General Shamsur Rahman Kallu, who showed it to Benazir the next day. The video tape showed the conversation of Major Amir Khan and Brigadier-General Imtiaz Ahmad revealed that Chief of Army Staff General Mirza Aslam Baig of that time wanted to end the government.
