Though the Brigadier failed to prove General Beg's involvement, General Mirza, on the other hand, sharply denied the accusation and started a full-fledged courts martial of these officers, with Benazir being the civilian judge of <a href="/wiki/Judge_Advocate_General_Branch" class="mw-redirect" title="Judge Advocate General Branch">JAG Branch</a> to proceed the hearings. The officers were removed from their positions and placed at <a href="/wiki/Adiala_Jail" class="mw-redirect" title="Adiala Jail">Adiala military correctional institute</a> in 1989. The officers were released from the military correctional institute by order of Prime Minister Nawaz Sharif in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-20090828DailyTimes_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20090828DailyTimes-141">[140]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dismissal">Dismissal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Dismissal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>By 1990 the revelation of Midnight Jackal lessened President Khan's influence in national politics, government and the military.<sup id="cite_ref-20090828DailyTimes_141-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20090828DailyTimes-141">[140]</a></sup> Bhutto was thought by the president to be a young and inexperienced figure in politics, though highly educated. But he miscalculated her capabilities; she emerged as a 'power player' in international politics. Bhutto's authoritative actions frustrated the president; he was not taken in confidence when decisions were made. By 1990 a power struggle between the prime minister and president ensued. Because of the semi-presidential system, Bhutto needed permission from Khan to impose new policies. Khan vetoed many, as he felt they contradicted his point of view. Bhutto, through her legislators, also attempted to shift to a <a href="/wiki/Parliamentary_democracy" class="mw-redirect" title="Parliamentary democracy">parliamentary democracy</a> from the semi-presidential system, but Khan always used his constitutional powers to veto Bhutto's attempts.<sup id="cite_ref-Lieven250_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lieven250-142">[141]</a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1980s, ethnic violence had broken out in Sindh province, with most of those killed being <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/muhajir" class="extiw" title="wikt:muhajir">mohajirs</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb1991138.E2.80.93139_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb1991138.E2.80.93139-143">[142]</a></sup> In May 1990, she ordered the army to restore peace in Karachi and Hyderabad.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb1991127_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb1991127-144">[143]</a></sup> Throughout her first term, Bhutto was criticised for being indecisive and unable to maintain control.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201361_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201361-145">[144]</a></sup> There had been anger that her husband had been allowed to attend cabinet meetings despite not being a member of the government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199172_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199172-92">[92]</a></sup> He was also accused of receiving <a href="/wiki/Kickbacks" class="mw-redirect" title="Kickbacks">kickbacks</a> and earned the nickname "Mr Ten Percent".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201361_145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201361-145">[144]</a></sup></p>
<p>Tales of corruption in public-sector industries began to surface, which undermined the credibility of Bhutto. The unemployment and labour strikes began to take place which halted and jammed the economic wheel of the country, and Bhutto was unable to solve these issues due to the cold war with the President.<sup id="cite_ref-Lieven250_142-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lieven250-142">[141]</a></sup> In August 1990, Khan dismissed Bhutto's government under the Eighth Amendment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201361_145-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201361-145">[144]</a></sup> He claimed that this was necessary due to her government's corruption and inability to maintain domestic law and order.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201361_145-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201361-145">[144]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="First_term_as_leader_of_the_opposition_(1990–93)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="First_term_as_leader_of_the_opposition_.281990.E2.80.9393.29">First term as leader of the opposition (1990–93)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: First term as leader of the opposition (1990–93)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Withbenazir2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8a/Withbenazir2.jpg/220px-Withbenazir2.jpg" width="220" height="277" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8a/Withbenazir2.jpg/330px-Withbenazir2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/8/8a/Withbenazir2.jpg/440px-Withbenazir2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1734" data-file-height="2184" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Withbenazir2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Benazir campaigning in <a href="/wiki/Lyari" title="Lyari">Lyari</a>, <a href="/wiki/Karachi" title="Karachi">Karachi</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Election_Commission_of_Pakistan" title="Election Commission of Pakistan">Election Commission of Pakistan</a> called for the new <a href="/wiki/Pakistani_general_election,_1990" title="Pakistani general election, 1990">parliamentary elections</a> in 1990.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In the election, the PPP only secured 45 of the 217 seats.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199117_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199117-146">[145]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Islami_Jamhoori_Ittehad" title="Islami Jamhoori Ittehad">Islami Jamhoori Ittehad</a>, or Islamic Democratic Alliance (IDA), under the leadership of Nawaz Sharif, won a majority in the Parliament; he became Prime Minister.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201362_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201362-147">[146]</a></sup> For the first time in the history of Pakistan, conservatives had a chance to rule the country.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Bhutto became the <a href="/wiki/Leader_of_the_Opposition_(Pakistan)" title="Leader of the Opposition (Pakistan)">leader of the opposition</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201362_147-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201362-147">[146]</a></sup> To journalists she remained unrependent about her time in office, insisting that she had made no mistakes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELamb199117_146-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELamb199117-146">[145]</a></sup></p>
<p>In November 1992, Bhutto attempted to perform a 10-mile march from Rawalpindi to Islamabad. However, she was forced to discontinue the rally due to a threat of arrest from Prime Minister Sharif.<sup id="cite_ref-148" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-148">[147]</a></sup> The demonstration was an anti-government rally that upset Pakistan officials.<sup id="cite_ref-149" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-149">[148]</a></sup> She was placed under house arrest and vowed to bring down the Pakistani government.<sup id="cite_ref-150" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-150">[149]</a></sup> In December 1992, a two-day march was conducted in protest of Nawaz Sharif.<sup id="cite_ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151">[150]</a></sup></p>
<p>After Bhutto's dismissal from government, her husband was arrested and charged with blackmail. According to the allegations, he had attached a remote-control bomb to the leg of a businessman and forced the latter to enter a bank and withdraw money.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200830.E2.80.9331_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200830.E2.80.9331-152">[151]</a></sup> He would be freed from prison after Bhutto's resumed the premiership in 1993.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200831_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200831-153">[152]</a></sup> Following the dismissal, the Bhutto couple purchased an apartment in the <a href="/wiki/Queen%27s_Gate" title="Queen's Gate">Queensgate</a> area of London.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008107_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008107-154">[153]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bhutto began to regularly attend lunches at the <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Institute_of_Development_Economics" title="Pakistan Institute of Development Economics">Institute of Development Economics</a> (IDE), a think tank founded in the 1950s; she had been visiting IDE and reading its publications since the mid-1970s. During that time, the IDA launched a secret campaign against Benazir Bhutto's image to demoralise party workers; the campaign brutally backfired on Nawaz Sharif when the media exposed the campaign and its motives.<sup id="cite_ref-MuhammadAhmadNoorani_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MuhammadAhmadNoorani-155">[154]</a></sup> More than ₨. 5 million were spent on the campaign and it undermined the credibility of conservatives, who also failed to resolve issues among between them.<sup id="cite_ref-MuhammadAhmadNoorani_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MuhammadAhmadNoorani-155">[154]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite an economic recovery in late 1993, the IDA government faced public unease about the direction of the country and an industrialisation that revolved around and centred only in Punjab Province. Amid protest and civil disorder in Sindh Province following the imposition of <a href="/wiki/Operation_Clean-up" title="Operation Clean-up">Operation Clean-up</a>, the IDA government lost control of the province.<sup id="cite_ref-156" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-156">[155]</a></sup> The Peoples Party attacked the IDA government's record on unemployment and industrial racism.<sup id="cite_ref-oppurtunity_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oppurtunity-157">[156]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sharif had attempted to reduce the president's powers.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201362_147-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201362-147">[146]</a></sup> With growing tensions between him and President Khan, in April 1993 the latter used the Eighth Amendment to dismiss Sharif as Prime Minister, citing corruption and misadministration.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201362_147-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201362-147">[146]</a></sup> Nawaz Sharif and Benazir Bhutto would unite to oust the president who lost the control of the country in a matter of weeks. Khan too was forced to resign along with Nawaz Sharif in 1993, and an interim government was formed until the new elections. A <a href="/wiki/Pakistani_general_election,_1993" title="Pakistani general election, 1993">parliamentary election</a> was called after by the <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Armed_Forces" title="Pakistan Armed Forces">Pakistan Armed Forces</a>. Both Sharif and Benazir Bhutto campaigned with full force, targeting each other's personalities.<sup id="cite_ref-elections_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-elections-158">[157]</a></sup> Their policies were very similar but a <a href="/wiki/Clash_of_personalities" class="mw-redirect" title="Clash of personalities">clash of personalities</a> occurred, with both parties making many promises but not explaining how they were going to pay for them.<sup id="cite_ref-tumult_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tumult-159">[158]</a></sup></p>
<p>Sharif stood on his record of privatisation and development, and pledged to restore his Taxicab|taxi giveaway program.<sup id="cite_ref-tumult_159-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tumult-159">[158]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-second_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-second-160">[159]</a></sup> Bhutto promised <a href="/wiki/Price_support" title="Price support">price supports</a> for agriculture, pledged a partnership between government and business, and campaigned strongly for the female vote.<sup id="cite_ref-second_160-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-second-160">[159]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Second_term_as_Prime_Minister_(1993–96)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_term_as_Prime_Minister_.281993.E2.80.9396.29">Second term as Prime Minister (1993–96)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Second term as Prime Minister (1993–96)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Benazir_Bhutto_with_Malik_Mehrun_Nisa_Afridi.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Benazir_Bhutto_with_Malik_Mehrun_Nisa_Afridi.tif/lossy-page1-220px-Benazir_Bhutto_with_Malik_Mehrun_Nisa_Afridi.tif.jpg" width="220" height="149" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Benazir_Bhutto_with_Malik_Mehrun_Nisa_Afridi.tif/lossy-page1-330px-Benazir_Bhutto_with_Malik_Mehrun_Nisa_Afridi.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Benazir_Bhutto_with_Malik_Mehrun_Nisa_Afridi.tif/lossy-page1-440px-Benazir_Bhutto_with_Malik_Mehrun_Nisa_Afridi.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2809" data-file-height="1899" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Benazir_Bhutto_with_Malik_Mehrun_Nisa_Afridi.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Benazir Bhutto with <a href="/wiki/Malik_Mehr_un_Nisa_Afridi" title="Malik Mehr un Nisa Afridi">Mehr un Nisa Afridi</a> at <a href="/wiki/Peshawar_Airport" class="mw-redirect" title="Peshawar Airport">Peshawar Airport</a> in 1993.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 1993 election, the PPP won the most seats,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200899_103-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200899-103">[103]</a></sup> although it fell short of an outright majority, with the PML-N in second place with 73 seats in the Parliament.<sup id="cite_ref-Moore_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore-161">[160]</a></sup> The PPP performed extremely well in Bhutto's native province, Sindh, and rural Punjab, while the PML-N was strongest in industrial Punjab and the largest cities such as Karachi, Lahore and Rawalpindi.<sup id="cite_ref-Gargan_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gargan-162">[161]</a></sup> On 19 October 1993, Benazir Bhutto was sworn as Prime Minister for second term allowing her to continue her reform initiatives.<sup id="cite_ref-Gargan_162-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gargan-162">[161]</a></sup> The following month, the PPP's nominee for the presidency, <a href="/wiki/Farooq_Leghari" title="Farooq Leghari">Farooq Leghari</a>, was also elected.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200899_103-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200899-103">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Moore_161-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore-161">[160]</a></sup></p>
<p>During her second term, Bhutto appointed both her husband and mother to her cabinet.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200830Mu.C3.B1oz201362_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200830Mu.C3.B1oz201362-163">[162]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Domestic_affairs">Domestic affairs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Domestic affairs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Benazir Bhutto was prime minister at a time of great racial tension in Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-19950601Dawn_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19950601Dawn-164">[163]</a></sup> Her approval poll rose by 38% after she appeared and said in a private television interview after the elections: "We are unhappy with the manner in which tampered electoral lists were provided in a majority of constituencies; our voters were turned away."<sup id="cite_ref-Gargan_162-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gargan-162">[161]</a></sup> The Conservatives attracted voters from <a href="/wiki/Muttahida_Majlis-e-Amal" title="Muttahida Majlis-e-Amal">religious society (MMA)</a> whose support had collapsed.<sup id="cite_ref-Gargan_162-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gargan-162">[161]</a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Friday_Times" class="mw-redirect" title="Friday Times">Friday Times</a></i> noted "Both of them (Nawaz and Benazir) have done so badly in the past, it will be very difficult for them to do worse now. If Bhutto's government fails, everyone knows there will be no new elections. The army will take over".<sup id="cite_ref-Moore_161-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Moore-161">[160]</a></sup> In confidential official documents Benazir Bhutto had objected to the number of <a href="/wiki/Urdu_speaking" class="mw-redirect" title="Urdu speaking">Urdu speaking class</a> in 1993 elections, in context that she had no Urdu-speaking sentiment in her circle and discrimination was continued even in her government. Her stance on these issues was perceived as part of rising public disclosure which <a href="/wiki/Altaf_Hussain_(Pakistani_politician)" title="Altaf Hussain (Pakistani politician)">Altaf Hussain</a> called "racism". Due to Benazir Bhutto's stubbornness and authoritative actions, her political rivals gave her the nickname "Iron Lady" of Pakistan. No response was issued by Bhutto, but she soon associated with the term.<sup id="cite_ref-19950601Dawn_164-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19950601Dawn-164">[163]</a></sup> The racial violence in Karachi was reached at peak and became a problem for Benazir Bhutto to counter. The MQM attempted to make an alliance with Benazir Bhutto under her own conditions, but Benazir Bhutto refused. Soon the second operation, <i><a href="/wiki/Operation_Blue_Fox" class="mw-redirect" title="Operation Blue Fox">Operation Blue Fox</a></i>, was launched to wipe the MQM from country's political spectrum. The results of this operation remain inconclusive and resulted in thousands killed or gone missing, with the majority being Urdu speaking.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Bhutto demanded the MQM to surrender to her government unconditionally. Though the operation was halted in 1995, but amid violence continued and, <a href="/wiki/Shahid_Javed_Burki" title="Shahid Javed Burki">Shahid Javed Burki</a>, a professor of economics, noted that "Karachi problem was not so much an ethnic problem as it was an economic question."<sup id="cite_ref-165" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-165">[164]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-166" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-166">[165]</a></sup> Amid union and labour strikes beginning to take place in Karachi and Lahore, which were encouraged by both Altaf Hussain and Nawaz Sharif to undermine her authority,<sup id="cite_ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167">[166]</a></sup> Benazir Bhutto responded by disbanding those trade union and issuing orders to arrest the leaders of the trade unions, while on the other hand, she provided incentives to local workers and labourers as she had separated the workers from their union leaders successfully.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Benazir Bhutto expanded the authoritative rights of <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Police" class="mw-redirect" title="Pakistan Police">Police Combatant Force</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Provinces_of_Pakistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Provinces of Pakistan">provisional governments</a> that tackled the local opposition aggressively. Bhutto, through her <a href="/wiki/Interior_Minister_of_Pakistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Interior Minister of Pakistan">Internal Security Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Naseerullah_Babar" title="Naseerullah Babar">Naseerullah Babar</a>, intensified the internal security operations and steps, gradually putting down the opposition's political rallies, while she did not completely abandon the reconciliation policy. In her own worlds, Benazir Bhutto announced: "There was no basis for (strikes)... in view of the ongoing political process...".<sup id="cite_ref-LawAndOrder_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-LawAndOrder-168">[167]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 1993, news began to surface in the <a href="/wiki/Swat_valley" class="mw-redirect" title="Swat valley">Swat valley</a> when <a href="/wiki/Sufi_Muhammad" title="Sufi Muhammad">Sufi Muhammad</a>, a religious cleric, began to mobilise the local militia calling for overthrow of the "un-Islamic rule of [Iron] Lady". Benazir Bhutto responded quickly and ordered the <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Army" title="Pakistan Army">Pakistan Army</a> to crack down n the militia, leading to the movement's being crushed by the Army and the cleric was apprehended before he could escape.<sup id="cite_ref-169" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-169">[168]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="Women's_issues"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Women.27s_issues">Women's issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Women's issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>During her election campaigns, she had promised to repeal controversial laws (such as <a href="/wiki/Hudood" class="mw-redirect" title="Hudood">Hudood</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zina_(Arabic)" class="mw-redirect" title="Zina (Arabic)">Zina</a> ordinances) that curtail the rights of women in Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170">[169]</a></sup> Bhutto was <a href="/wiki/Pro-life" class="mw-redirect" title="Pro-life">pro-life</a> and spoke forcefully against abortion, most notably at the <a href="/wiki/International_Conference_on_Population_and_Development" title="International Conference on Population and Development">International Conference on Population and Development</a> in Cairo, where she accused the West of "seeking to impose adultery, abortion, intercourse education and other such matters on individuals, societies and religions which have their own social ethos."<sup id="cite_ref-171" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-171">[170]</a></sup> However, Bhutto was not supported by the leading women organisations, who argued that after being elected twice, none of the reforms were made, instead controversial laws were exercised more toughly. Therefore, in 1997 elections, Bhutto failed to secure any support from women's organisations and minorities also gave Bhutto the cold-shoulder when she approached them. It was not until 2006 that the Zina ordinance was finally repealed by a Presidential Ordinance issued by <a href="/wiki/Pervez_Musharraf" title="Pervez Musharraf">Pervez Musharraf</a> in July 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-172" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-172">[171]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bhutto was a founding member of the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_Women_World_Leaders" title="Council of Women World Leaders">Council of Women World Leaders</a>, a group established in 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201364_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201364-173">[172]</a></sup> She appointed women judges and a women's division in the government, headed by a senior female civil servant.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008100.E2.80.93101_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008100.E2.80.93101-174">[173]</a></sup> She also oversaw the establishment of a women's bank and all-female police stations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008100.E2.80.93101_174-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008100.E2.80.93101-174">[173]</a></sup> The fundamentalist Islamic laws introduced to restrict women's rights under Zia nevertheless remained in place,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008101_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008101-175">[174]</a></sup> and no new legislation was put in place to empower women.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201364_173-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201364-173">[172]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_issues">Economic issues</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Economic issues">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Per_capita_GDP_of_South_Asian_economies_%26_SKorea_(1950-1995).png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Per_capita_GDP_of_South_Asian_economies_%26_SKorea_%281950-1995%29.png/250px-Per_capita_GDP_of_South_Asian_economies_%26_SKorea_%281950-1995%29.png" width="250" height="151" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Per_capita_GDP_of_South_Asian_economies_%26_SKorea_%281950-1995%29.png/375px-Per_capita_GDP_of_South_Asian_economies_%26_SKorea_%281950-1995%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Per_capita_GDP_of_South_Asian_economies_%26_SKorea_%281950-1995%29.png/500px-Per_capita_GDP_of_South_Asian_economies_%26_SKorea_%281950-1995%29.png 2x" data-file-width="769" data-file-height="464" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Per_capita_GDP_of_South_Asian_economies_%26_SKorea_(1950-1995).png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The total <a href="/wiki/GDP_per_capita" class="mw-redirect" title="GDP per capita">GDP per capita</a> as a percentage of the U.S. GDP per capita stood between 8.4% (in the 1970s) and 8.3% (in 1993–96), periods of nationalisation.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Bhutto was an economist by profession; therefore during her terms as prime minister, she herself took charge of the <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Finance_(Pakistan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ministry of Finance (Pakistan)">Ministry of Finance</a>. Bhutto sought to improve the country's economy which was declining as time was passing. Benazir disagreed with her father's nationalization and <a href="/wiki/Socialist_economics" title="Socialist economics">socialist economics</a>. Soon after the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a>, Benazir attempted to privatize major industries that were nationalized in the 1970s.<sup id="cite_ref-Routledge_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Routledge-176">[175]</a></sup> Bhutto promised to end the nationalisation programme and to carry out the industrialisation programme by means other than state intervention. But controversially Bhutto did not carry out the <a href="/wiki/Denationalization" class="mw-redirect" title="Denationalization">denationalization</a> programme or liberalization of the economy during her first government. No nationalized units were privatized, few economic regulations were reviewed.<sup id="cite_ref-177" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-177">[176]</a></sup></p>
<p>Pakistan suffered a currency crisis when the government failed to arrest the 30% fall in the value of the <a href="/wiki/Pakistani_Rupee" class="mw-redirect" title="Pakistani Rupee">Pakistani Rupee</a> from ₨. 21 to ₨.30 compared to the United States dollar. Soon economic progress became her top priority but her investment and industrialisation programs faced major setbacks due to conceptions formed by investors based upon her People's Party nationalisation program in the 1970s. By the 1990s, Khan and Bhutto's government had also ultimately lost the <a href="/wiki/Currency_war" title="Currency war">currency war</a> with the Indian Rupee which beat the value of Pakistan rupee for the first time in the 1970s. Bhutto's denationalisation program also suffered from many political setbacks, as many of her government members were either directly or indirectly involved with the government corruption in major government-owned industries, and her appointed government members allegedly sabotaged her efforts to privatise the industries.<sup id="cite_ref-Routledge_176-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Routledge-176">[175]</a></sup></p>
<p>Overall, the living standard for people in Pakistan declined as inflation and unemployment grew at an exponential rate particularly as UN sanctions began to take effect. During her first and second term, the difference between rich and poor visibly increased and the middle class in particular were the ones who bore the brunt of the economic inequality. According to a calculation completed by the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Statistics" class="mw-redirect" title="Federal Bureau of Statistics">Federal Bureau of Statistics</a>, the rich were statistically were improved and the poor declined in terms of living standards.<sup id="cite_ref-Routledge_176-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Routledge-176">[175]</a></sup> Benazir attributed this economic inequality to be a result of ongoing and continuous illegal <a href="/wiki/Bangladeshis_in_Pakistan" class="mw-redirect" title="Bangladeshis in Pakistan">Bangladeshi immigration</a>. Bhutto ordered a crackdown on and deportation of illegal Bangladeshi immigrants. Her action strained and created tensions in <a href="/wiki/Bangladesh%E2%80%93Pakistan_relations" title="Bangladesh–Pakistan relations">Bangladesh–Pakistan relations</a>, as Bangladesh Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Khaleda_Zia" title="Khaleda Zia">Khaleda Zia</a> refused to accept the deportees and reportedly sent two planeloads back to Pakistan. Religious parties also criticised Bhutto and dubbed the crackdown as anti-Islamic.<sup id="cite_ref-Pakistandirectorate_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pakistandirectorate-178">[177]</a></sup></p>
<p>This operation backfired and had devastating effects on Pakistan's economy.<sup id="cite_ref-Pakistandirectorate_178-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pakistandirectorate-178">[177]</a></sup> President Khan saw this as a major economic failure despite Khan's permission granted to Bhutto for the approval of her economic policies. Khan blamed Bhutto for this extensive economic slowdown and her policy that failed to stop the illegal immigration. Khan attributed Bhutto's government members corruption in government-owned industries as the major sink hole in Pakistan's economy that failed to compete with neighbouring India's economy.<sup id="cite_ref-Routledge_176-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Routledge-176">[175]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Privatization_and_era_of_stagflation">Privatization and era of stagflation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Privatization and era of stagflation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Pakistan_gdp_growth_rate.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Pakistan_gdp_growth_rate.svg/250px-Pakistan_gdp_growth_rate.svg.png" width="250" height="123" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Pakistan_gdp_growth_rate.svg/375px-Pakistan_gdp_growth_rate.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Pakistan_gdp_growth_rate.svg/500px-Pakistan_gdp_growth_rate.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="813" data-file-height="400" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Pakistan_gdp_growth_rate.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/GDP_growth" class="mw-redirect" title="GDP growth">GDP growth</a> rate was at ~4.37% in 1993, which fell to ~1.70% in 1996, before Bhutto's dismissal.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During her second term, Bhutto continued to follow former prime minister Nawaz Sharif's <a href="/wiki/Privatisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Privatisation">privatisation</a> policies, which she called a "disciplined macroeconomics policy". After the 1993 general elections, the privatisation programme of state-owned banks and utilities accelerated; more than ₨ 42 billion was raised from the sale of nationalised corporations and industries, and another US$20 billion from the foreign investment made the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Dawn2_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dawn2-179">[178]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180">[179]</a></sup> After 1993, the country's national economy again entered in the second period of the stagflation and more roughly began bite the country's financial resources and the <a href="/wiki/Financial_capital" title="Financial capital">financial capital</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-SartajAziz_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SartajAziz-181">[180]</a></sup> Bhutto's second government found it extremely difficult to counter the <a href="/wiki/Periods_of_stagflation_in_Pakistan" title="Periods of stagflation in Pakistan">second era of stagflation</a> with Pressler amendment and the US financial and military embargo tightened its position.<sup id="cite_ref-SartajAziz_181-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SartajAziz-181">[180]</a></sup> After a year of study, Bhutto implemented and enforced the <a href="/wiki/Eighth_Five-Year_Plans_(Pakistan)" title="Eighth Five-Year Plans (Pakistan)">Eighth Plan</a> to overcome the stagflation by creating a dependable and effective mechanism for accelerating economic and social progress. But, according to American ambassador to Pakistan, <a href="/wiki/William_Milam" title="William Milam">William Milam</a>'s bibliography, <i>Bangladesh and Pakistan:Flirting with Failure in South Asia</i>, the Eighth Plan (which reflected the <a href="/wiki/Planned_economy_of_the_Soviet_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Planned economy of the Soviet Union">planned economy of the Soviet Union</a>) was doomed to meet with failure from the very beginning of 1994, as the policies were weak and incoherent.<sup id="cite_ref-182" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-182">[181]</a></sup></p>
<p>On many occasions, Bhutto resisted to privatise globally competitive and billion-dollar-worth state-owned enterprises (such as <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Railways" title="Pakistan Railways">Pakistan Railways</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Steel_Mills" title="Pakistan Steel Mills">Pakistan Steel Mills</a>), instead the grip of nationalisation in those state-owned enterprises was tightened in order to secure the <a href="/wiki/Capital_investment" class="mw-redirect" title="Capital investment">capital investment</a> of these industries. The process of privatisation of the nationalised industries was associated with the marked performance and improvement, especially the terms of <a href="/wiki/Labour_productivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Labour productivity">labour productivity</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Dawn2_179-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dawn2-179">[178]</a></sup> A number of privatization of industries such as gas, <a href="/wiki/Water_supply_and_sanitation_in_Pakistan" title="Water supply and sanitation in Pakistan">water supply and sanitation</a>, and electricity general, were <a href="/wiki/Natural_monopoly" title="Natural monopoly">natural monopolies</a> for which the privatization involved little competition.<sup id="cite_ref-Dawn2_179-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dawn2-179">[178]</a></sup> Furthermore, Benazir denied that privatisation of the Pakistan Railways would take place despite the calls made in Pakistan, and was said to have told <a href="/wiki/Planning_Commission_(Pakistan)" title="Planning Commission (Pakistan)">Planning Commission</a> chief <a href="/wiki/Naveed_Qamar" title="Naveed Qamar">Naveed Qamar</a>, "Railways privatization will be the 'blackhole' of this government. Please never mention the railways to me again". Bhutto always resisted privatisation of <a href="/wiki/United_Bank_Limited_Pakistan" class="mw-redirect" title="United Bank Limited Pakistan">United Bank Limited Pakistan</a> (UBL), but its management sent the recommendation for the privatisation which dismayed the labour union. The United Group of Employees Management asked Bhutto for issue of regulation sheet which she denied. The holding of UBL in government control turned out to be a move that ended in "disaster" for Bhutto's government.<sup id="cite_ref-183" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-183">[182]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_policy_2">Foreign policy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Foreign policy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Benazir Bhutto's foreign policy was controversial. In her second term, Bhutto expanded Pakistan's relations with the rest of the world. Like her father, Benazir Bhutto sought to strengthen relations with <a href="/wiki/List_of_socialist_countries" class="mw-redirect" title="List of socialist countries">socialist states</a>, and her visit to <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> <a href="/wiki/Libya%E2%80%93Pakistan_relations" title="Libya–Pakistan relations">strengthened the relations</a> between the two countries. Benazir also thanked <a href="/wiki/Muammar_al-Gaddafi" class="mw-redirect" title="Muammar al-Gaddafi">Muammar al-Gaddafi</a> for his tremendous efforts and support for her father before and during Zulfikar's trial in 1977. Ties continued with Libya but deteriorated after Nawaz Sharif became prime minister in 1990 and again in 1997. Gaddafi was said to be very fond of Bhutto and was a family friend of <a href="/wiki/Bhutto_family" title="Bhutto family">Bhutto family</a>, but disliked Nawaz Sharif due to his ties with General Zia in the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-184" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-184">[183]</a></sup></p>
<p>Benazir Bhutto is said to have paid a state visit to North Korea in early 1990 and again in 1996. According to journalist Shyam Bhatia, Bhutto smuggled CDs containing <a href="/wiki/Uranium_enrichment" class="mw-redirect" title="Uranium enrichment">uranium enrichment</a> data to North Korea on a state visit that same year<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Avoid_weasel_words" class="mw-redirect" title="Wikipedia:Avoid weasel words"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too vague attribution or weasel words. (May 2016)">which?</span></a></i>]</sup> in return for data on missile technology.<sup id="cite_ref-185" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-185">[184]</a></sup> According to the expert<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (May 2016)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup>, Benazir Bhutto acted as a female "<a href="/wiki/James_Bond" title="James Bond">James Bond</a>", and left with a bag of computer disks to pass on to her military from North Korea.<sup id="cite_ref-DavidIsenber_127-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DavidIsenber-127">[127]</a></sup> Benazir Bhutto is described as a political "architect of Pakistan's missile technology" by Emily MacFarquhar of <a href="/wiki/Alicia_Patterson_Foundation" title="Alicia Patterson Foundation">Alicia Patterson Foundation</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Alicia_Patterson_Foundation_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Alicia_Patterson_Foundation-186">[185]</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/Socialism_in_Pakistan" title="Socialism in Pakistan">leftist convention</a> held in 2014, former <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Pakistan" title="Prime Minister of Pakistan">Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Yousaf_Raza_Gillani" title="Yousaf Raza Gillani">Yousaf Raza Gillani</a> said, "Benazir Bhutto gave this country the much-needed missile technology".<sup id="cite_ref-GEO_TV_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-GEO_TV-187">[186]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Prime_Minister_Benazir_Bhutto_1988.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Prime_Minister_Benazir_Bhutto_1988.jpg/220px-Prime_Minister_Benazir_Bhutto_1988.jpg" width="220" height="148" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Prime_Minister_Benazir_Bhutto_1988.jpg/330px-Prime_Minister_Benazir_Bhutto_1988.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/Prime_Minister_Benazir_Bhutto_1988.jpg/440px-Prime_Minister_Benazir_Bhutto_1988.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2830" data-file-height="1900" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Prime_Minister_Benazir_Bhutto_1988.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Benazir Bhutto in the United States, 1989</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Major-General Pervez Musharraf worked closely with Bhutto and her government in formulating an Israel strategy. In 1993 Bhutto ordered Musharraf, then Director-General of the Pakistani Army's Directorate-General for the Military Operation (DGMO), to join her state visit to the United States, an unusual and unconventional participation. Bhutto and Musharraf chaired a secret meeting with Israeli officials who travelled to the US especially for the meeting. Under Bhutto's guidance Musharraf intensified the ISI's liaison with Israel's <a href="/wiki/Mossad" title="Mossad">Mossad</a>. A final meeting took place in 1995, which Musharraf also joined.<sup id="cite_ref-188" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-188">[187]</a></sup> Bhutto also strengthened <a href="/wiki/Pakistan%E2%80%93Vietnam_relations" title="Pakistan–Vietnam relations">relations</a> with communist <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, and visited Vietnam<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The time period mentioned near this tag is ambiguous. (May 2016)">when?</span></a></i>]</sup> to sign an agreement for mutual trade and international political cooperation the two countries. In 1995 Benazir Bhutto made another state visit to the United States and held talks with U.S. President <a href="/wiki/Bill_Clinton" title="Bill Clinton">Bill Clinton</a>. Bhutto urged him to revise the <a href="/wiki/Pressler_Amendment" class="mw-redirect" title="Pressler Amendment">Pressler Amendment</a> and launch a campaign against extremism. She criticized US nonproliferation policy and demanded that the United States honour its contractual obligation.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (May 2016)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup id="cite_ref-Dawn1_135-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dawn1-135">[135]</a></sup></p>
<p>During her second term, relations with Indian Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/P._V._Narasimha_Rao" title="P. V. Narasimha Rao">P. V. Narasimha Rao</a> further deteriorated. Like her father, Benazir Bhutto used rhetoric to oppose to India and campaign in the international community against the Indian nuclear programme. On 1 May 1995 she used harsh language in her public warning to India that "continuation of [Indian] nuclear programme would have terrible consequences".<sup id="cite_ref-NTI_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NTI-189">[188]</a></sup> India responded to this saying she was interfering in an "internal matter" of India, and the Indian Army fired a <a href="/wiki/Rocket-propelled_grenade" title="Rocket-propelled grenade">RPG</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Kahuta" title="Kahuta">Kahuta</a>, which further escalated events, leading to full-fledged war.<sup id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-190">[189]</a></sup> When this news reached Bhutto, she responded by high-alerting the <a href="/wiki/Air_Force_Strategic_Command_(Pakistan)" title="Air Force Strategic Command (Pakistan)">Air Force Strategic Command</a>. It ordered heavily armed <a href="/wiki/No._11_Squadron_Arrows" class="mw-redirect" title="No. 11 Squadron Arrows">Arrows</a>, <a href="/wiki/No._9_Squadron_Griffins" class="mw-redirect" title="No. 9 Squadron Griffins">Griffins</a>, <a href="/wiki/No._26_Squadron_(Pakistan_Air_Force)" title="No. 26 Squadron (Pakistan Air Force)">Black Panthers</a> and the <a href="/wiki/No._26_Squadron_(Pakistan_Air_Force)" title="No. 26 Squadron (Pakistan Air Force)">Black Spiders</a> to begin air sorties and to patrol the Indo-Pakistan border on day-and-night regular missions. All of these squadrons are part of the Strategic Command. On 30 May, India test-fired a <a href="/wiki/Prithvi_(missile)" title="Prithvi (missile)">Prithvi-1</a> missile near the Pakistan border, which Bhutto condemned. She responded by deploying <a href="/wiki/Shaheen-I" title="Shaheen-I">Shaheen-I</a> missiles; however, they were not armed. Benazir Bhutto permitted the <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Air_Force" title="Pakistan Air Force">PAF</a> to deploy the <a href="/wiki/Crotale_(missile)" title="Crotale (missile)">Crotale missile defence</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Anza_(missile)" title="Anza (missile)">Anza-Mk-III</a> near the Indian border, which escalated the conflict, but effectively kept the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Army" title="Indian Army">Indian Army</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Air_Force" title="Indian Air Force">Indian Air Force</a> from launching any surprise attack.<sup id="cite_ref-19950601Dawn_164-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19950601Dawn-164">[163]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1995 the ISI reported to Bhutto that Narasimha Rao had authorised nuclear tests, and that they could be conducted at any minute.<sup id="cite_ref-19950601Dawn_164-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19950601Dawn-164">[163]</a></sup> Benazir put the country's nuclear arsenal programme on high-alert<sup id="cite_ref-191" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-191">[190]</a></sup> made emergency preparations, and ordered the Pakistani armed forces to remain on high-alert.<sup id="cite_ref-NTI_189-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NTI-189">[188]</a></sup> However the United States intervened, Indian operations for conducting the nuclear tests were called off and the Japanese government attempted to mediate. In 1996, Benazir Bhutto met with Japanese officials and warned India about conducting nuclear tests. She revealed for the first time that Pakistan had achieved parity with India in its capacity to produce nuclear weapons and their delivery capability. She told the Indian press, that Pakistan "cannot afford to negate the parity we maintain with India". These statements represented a departure from Pakistan's previous policy of "nuclear ambivalence."<sup id="cite_ref-NTI_189-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NTI-189">[188]</a></sup> Bhutto issued a statement on the tests and told the international press that she condemned the Indian nuclear tests. "If (India) conducts a nuclear test, it would forced her (Pakistan) to.. "follow suit..." she said.<sup id="cite_ref-NTI_189-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NTI-189">[188]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bhutto also ratcheted up her policy on Indian <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmir</a>, rallying against India.<sup id="cite_ref-19950905Dawn_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19950905Dawn-192">[191]</a></sup> At an <a href="/wiki/Inter-Parliamentary_Union" title="Inter-Parliamentary Union">Inter-Parliamentary Union</a> meeting at the United Nations, Bhutto, who was accompanied by her <a href="/wiki/Speaker_of_the_National_Assembly_of_Pakistan" title="Speaker of the National Assembly of Pakistan">Speaker</a> <a href="/wiki/Yousaf_Raza_Gillani" title="Yousaf Raza Gillani">Yousaf Raza Gillani</a> upset and angered the Indian delegation, headed by prime minister <a href="/wiki/Atal_Bihari_Vajpayee" title="Atal Bihari Vajpayee">Atal Bihari Vajpayee</a>, with a vehement criticism of India.<sup id="cite_ref-19950905Dawn_192-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19950905Dawn-192">[191]</a></sup> Vajpayee responded, saying: "It is Pakistan which is flouting the United Nations resolution by not withdrawing its forces from Kashmir...You people create problems every time. You know the Kashmiri people themselves acceded to India. First, the Maharajah, then the Kashmiri parliament, both decided to go with India".<sup id="cite_ref-19950905Dawn_192-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19950905Dawn-192">[191]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bhutto described Indian held-Kashmir as the worst example of "Indian intransigence" and dismissed Indian allegations of <a href="/wiki/List_of_nuclear_weapons_tests#Pakistan" title="List of nuclear weapons tests">putative Pakistani nuclear tests</a> as "baseless". Bhutto criticised India's bid to hide its plan to explode a nuclear device, and failure to cover up its domestic problems including its failure to suppress the freedom struggle in Kashmir.<sup id="cite_ref-193" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-193">[192]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Relations_with_military">Relations with military</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Relations with military">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:SIC-_Benazir_Bhutto_%26_Malik_Mehrun_Nisa_Afridi_02.tif" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/SIC-_Benazir_Bhutto_%26_Malik_Mehrun_Nisa_Afridi_02.tif/lossy-page1-220px-SIC-_Benazir_Bhutto_%26_Malik_Mehrun_Nisa_Afridi_02.tif.jpg" width="220" height="152" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/SIC-_Benazir_Bhutto_%26_Malik_Mehrun_Nisa_Afridi_02.tif/lossy-page1-330px-SIC-_Benazir_Bhutto_%26_Malik_Mehrun_Nisa_Afridi_02.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/SIC-_Benazir_Bhutto_%26_Malik_Mehrun_Nisa_Afridi_02.tif/lossy-page1-440px-SIC-_Benazir_Bhutto_%26_Malik_Mehrun_Nisa_Afridi_02.tif.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2445" data-file-height="1689" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:SIC-_Benazir_Bhutto_%26_Malik_Mehrun_Nisa_Afridi_02.tif" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Benazir at Social International's 50th Anniversary held at Lisbon, Portugal.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During her first term, Benazir Bhutto had strained relationship with the Pakistan Armed Forces, especially with Pakistan Army. Army chief Mirza Aslam Beg had cold relations with the elected prime minister, and continued to undermine her authority. As for the military appointments, Benazir Bhutto refused to appoint General Beg as the <a href="/wiki/Chairman_Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff_Committee_(Pakistan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Chairman Joint Chiefs of Staff Committee (Pakistan)">Chairman</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Joint_Chiefs_of_Staff_Committee" title="Joint Chiefs of Staff Committee">Joint Chiefs of Staff Committee</a>, instead invited Admiral <a href="/wiki/Iftikhar_Ahmed_Sirohey" title="Iftikhar Ahmed Sirohey">Iftikhar Ahmed Sirohey</a> to take the chairmanship of the Joint Chiefs of Staff.<sup id="cite_ref-194" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-194">[193]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-195" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-195">[194]</a></sup> In 1988, Benazir Bhutto appointed Air Chief Marshal <a href="/wiki/Hakimullah" class="mw-redirect" title="Hakimullah">Hakimullah</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Air_Staff_(Pakistan)" title="Chief of Air Staff (Pakistan)">Chief of Air Staff</a> and Admiral <a href="/wiki/Yastur-ul-Haq_Malik" title="Yastur-ul-Haq Malik">Jastural Haq</a> as the <a href="/wiki/Chief_of_Naval_Staff_(Pakistan)" title="Chief of Naval Staff (Pakistan)">Chief of Naval Staff</a>. In 1988, shortly after assuming the office, Benazir Bhutto paid a visit to <a href="/wiki/Siachen" class="mw-redirect" title="Siachen">Siachen</a> region, to boost the moral of the soldiers who fought the <a href="/wiki/Siachen_conflict" title="Siachen conflict">Siachen war</a> with India. This was the first visit of any civilian leader to any military war-zone area since the country's independence in 1947.<sup id="cite_ref-196" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-196">[195]</a></sup> In 1988, Benazir appointed Major-General Pervez Musharraf as Director-General of the Army Directorate General for Military Operations (DGMO); and then-Brigadier-General <a href="/wiki/Ashfaq_Parvez_Kayani" title="Ashfaq Parvez Kayani">Ashfaq Parvez Kayani</a> as her Military-Secretary.<sup id="cite_ref-197" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-197">[196]</a></sup> In 1989, the Pakistan Army exposed the alleged Operation Midnight Jackal against the government of Benazir Bhutto. When she learned the news, Benazir Bhutto ordered the arrest and trial of former <i>ISI</i> officer Brigadier Imtiaz Ahmad and Major Amir Khan, it was later revealed that it was General Beg who was behind this plot. General Beg soon paid the price in 1993 elections, when Benazir Bhutto politically destroyed the former general and his career was over before taking any shifts in politics.<sup id="cite_ref-198" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-198">[197]</a></sup> During her first term, Benazir Bhutto had successfully removed senior military officers including Lieutenant-Generals Hamid Gul, <a href="/wiki/Zahid_Ali_Akbar_Khan" title="Zahid Ali Akbar Khan">Zahid Ali Akbar Khan</a>, General <a href="/wiki/Jamal_A._Khan" title="Jamal A. Khan">Jamal A. Khan</a>, and Admiral <a href="/wiki/Tariq_Kamal_Khan" title="Tariq Kamal Khan">Tariq Kamal Khan</a>, all of whom had anti-democratic views and were closely aligned to General Zia, replacing them with officers who were educated in Western military institutes and academies, generally the ones with more westernised democratic views.<sup id="cite_ref-Mazhar_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mazhar-199">[198]</a></sup></p>
<p>During her second term, Benazir Bhutto's relations with the Pakistan Armed Forces took a different and pro-Bhutto approach, when she carefully appointed General <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Waheed_Kakar" title="Abdul Waheed Kakar">Abdul Waheed Kakar</a> as the Chief of Army Staff. General Abdul Waheed was an uptight, strict, and a professional officer with a views of Westernized democracy. Benazir also appointed Admiral <a href="/wiki/Saeed_Mohammad_Khan" title="Saeed Mohammad Khan">Saeed Mohammad Khan</a> as Chief of Naval Staff; General <a href="/wiki/Abbas_Khattak" title="Abbas Khattak">Abbas Khattak</a> as Chief of Air Staff. Whilst, Air Chief Marshal <a href="/wiki/Farooq_Feroze_Khan" title="Farooq Feroze Khan">Farooq Feroze Khan</a> was appointed chairman Joint Chiefs who was the first (and to date only) Pakistani <a href="/wiki/Air_officer" title="Air officer">air officer</a> to have reached to such <a href="/wiki/4-star_rank" class="mw-redirect" title="4-star rank">4 star</a> assignment. Benazir Bhutto enjoyed a strong relations with the Pakistan Armed Forces, and President who was hand-picked by her did not questioned her authority. She hand-picked officers and promoted them based on their pro-democracy views while the puppet President gave constitutional authorisation for their promotion. The senior military leadership including <a href="/wiki/Jehangir_Karamat" title="Jehangir Karamat">Jehangir Karamat</a>, Musharraf, Kayani, <a href="/wiki/Ali_Kuli_Khan" class="mw-redirect" title="Ali Kuli Khan">Ali Kuli Khan</a>, Farooq Feroze Khan, Abbas Khattak and <a href="/wiki/Fasih_Bokhari" title="Fasih Bokhari">Fasih Bokhari</a>, had strong Western-democratic views, and were generally close to Bhutto as they had resisted Nawaz Sharif's conservatism. Unlike Nawaz Sharif's second democratic term, Benazir worked with the military on many issues where the military disagreement, solving many problems relating directly to civil–military relations. Her tough and hardline policies on Afghanistan, Kashmir and India, which the military had backed Benazir Bhutto staunchly.<sup id="cite_ref-Mazhar_199-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mazhar-199">[198]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the assassination was attempted, Benazir Bhutto's civilian security team headed under <a href="/wiki/Rehman_Malik" title="Rehman Malik">Rehman Malik</a>, was disbanded by the Pakistan Army whose <a href="/wiki/X_Corps_(Pakistan)" title="X Corps (Pakistan)">X-Corps</a>' <a href="/wiki/111th_Infantry_Brigade_(Pakistan)" title="111th Infantry Brigade (Pakistan)">111th <i>Psychological</i> Brigade</a>— an army brigade tasked with countering the <a href="/wiki/Psychological_warfare" title="Psychological warfare">psychological warfare</a>— took control of the security of Benazir Bhutto, that directly reported to Chief of Army Staff and the Prime Minister. Benazir Bhutto ordered General Abdul Waheed Kakar and the Lieutenant-General <a href="/wiki/Javed_Ashraf_Qazi" title="Javed Ashraf Qazi">Javed Ashraf Qazi</a> director-general of <a href="/wiki/ISI_(Pakistan)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISI (Pakistan)">ISI</a>, to start a sting and manhunt operation to hunt down the ringmaster, <a href="/wiki/Ramzi_Yousef" title="Ramzi Yousef">Ramzi Yousef</a>. After few arrests and intensive manhunt search, the <i>ISI</i> finally captured Ramzi before he could flew the country. In matter of weeks, Ramzi was secretly extradited to the United States, while the <i>ISI</i> managed to kill or apprehend all the culprits behind the plot. In 1995, she personally appointed General <a href="/wiki/Naseem_Rana" title="Naseem Rana">Naseem Rana</a> as the Director-General of the <i>ISI</i>, who later commanded the Pakistan Army's assets in which came to known as "<a href="/wiki/Civil_war_in_Afghanistan_(1996%E2%80%932001)" class="mw-redirect" title="Civil war in Afghanistan (1996–2001)">Pakistan's secret war in Afghanistan</a>". During this course, General Rana directly reported to the prime minister, and led the intelligence operations after which were approved by Benazir Bhutto. In 1995, Benazir also appointed Admiral <a href="/wiki/Mansurul_Haq" title="Mansurul Haq">Mansurul Haq</a> as the Chief of Naval Staff, as the Admiral had personal contacts with the Benazir's family. However, it was the Admiral's large-scale corruption, sponsored by her husband <a href="/wiki/Asif_Zardari" class="mw-redirect" title="Asif Zardari">Asif Zardari</a>, that shrunk the credibility of Benazir Bhutto by the end of 1996 that led to end of her government after all.<sup id="cite_ref-Mazhar_199-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mazhar-199">[198]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Policy_on_Taliban">Policy on Taliban</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Policy on Taliban">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>1996 was crucial for Bhutto's policy on Afghanistan when Pakistan-backed extremely religious group <a href="/wiki/Taliban" title="Taliban">Taliban</a> took power in Kabul in September.<sup id="cite_ref-legacy_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-legacy-200">[199]</a></sup> She continued her father's policy on Afghanistan taking aggressive measures to curb the anti-Pakistan sentiments in Afghanistan. During this time, many in the international community at the time, including the United States government, viewed the Taliban as a group that could stabilise Afghanistan and enable trade access to the <a href="/wiki/Central_Asian_Republics" class="mw-redirect" title="Central Asian Republics">Central Asian Republics</a>, according to author <a href="/wiki/Steve_Coll" title="Steve Coll">Steve Coll</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-201" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-201">[200]</a></sup></p>
<p>He claims that her government provided military and financial support for the Taliban, even sending a small unit of the <a href="/wiki/Pakistan_Army" title="Pakistan Army">Pakistan Army</a> into Afghanistan. Benazir had approved the appointment of Lieutenant-General <a href="/wiki/Naseem_Rana" title="Naseem Rana">Naseem Rana</a> who she affectionately referred to him as "<a href="/wiki/Georgy_Zhukov" title="Georgy Zhukov">Georgy Zhukov</a>"; and had reported to her while providing strategic support to Taliban. During her regime, Benazir Bhutto's government had controversially supported the hardline Taliban, and many of her government officials were providing financial assistance to the Taliban.<sup id="cite_ref-legacy_200-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-legacy-200">[199]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fazal-ur-Rehman_(politician)" class="mw-redirect" title="Fazal-ur-Rehman (politician)">Fazal-ur-Rehman</a>, a right-wing cleric, had a traditionally deep influence on Bhutto as he convinced and later assisted her to help the regime of Taliban she established the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Emirate_of_Afghanistan" title="Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan">Islamic Emirate of Afghanistan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-202" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-202">[201]</a></sup> In a reference written by American scholar, <a href="/wiki/Steve_Coll" title="Steve Coll">Steve Coll</a> in <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Ghost_Wars:_The_Secret_History_of_the_CIA,_Afghanistan,_and_Bin_Laden,_from_the_Soviet_Invasion_to_10_September_2001&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Ghost Wars: The Secret History of the CIA, Afghanistan, and Bin Laden, from the Soviet Invasion to 10 September 2001 (page does not exist)">Ghost Wars</a></i>, he dryly put it: "Benazir Bhutto was suddenly the matron of a new Afghan faction—the Taliban."<sup id="cite_ref-Star.com_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Star.com-203">[202]</a></sup></p>
<p>Under her government, Pakistan had recognised the Taliban regime as legitimate government in Afghanistan, allowing the Taliban to open an <a href="/wiki/Embassy_of_Afghanistan,_Islamabad" title="Embassy of Afghanistan, Islamabad">embassy</a> in Islamabad. In 1996, the newly appointed <a href="/wiki/Foreign_relations_of_Afghanistan" title="Foreign relations of Afghanistan">Afghan Ambassador to Pakistan</a> <a href="/wiki/Mullah" title="Mullah">Mullah</a> <a href="/wiki/Abdul_Salam_Zaeef" title="Abdul Salam Zaeef">Abdul Salam Zaeef</a> presented her <a href="/wiki/Diplomatic_credentials" title="Diplomatic credentials">diplomatic credentials</a> However, in 2007, she took an anti-Taliban stance, and condemned terrorist acts allegedly committed by the Taliban and their supporters.<sup id="cite_ref-204" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-204">[203]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_of_younger_brother">Death of younger brother</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Death of younger brother">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1993, Murtaza returned to Pakistan, where he gained a following within the PPP. He claimed that it was he, rather than his sister, who was the standard bearer for their father's championing of the downtrodden.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200825Mu.C3.B1oz201364_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200825Mu.C3.B1oz201364-205">[204]</a></sup> On arrival, he was arrested for his part in the earlier plane hijacking and remained imprisoned for eight months.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201364_173-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201364-173">[172]</a></sup> In 1995, he established his own party, the PPP (Shaheed Bhutto).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201365_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201365-206">[205]</a></sup> Nusrat assisted him in organising Murtaza's election campaign for the Sindh provincial assembly.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008102_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008102-207">[206]</a></sup> Bhutto was so angry with her mother's actions that she ousted her as co-chair of the PPP.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008102_207-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008102-207">[206]</a></sup> Murtaza focused much of his criticism on Bhutto's husband Zardari, whom he blamed of being responsible for government corruption.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200826_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200826-208">[207]</a></sup> It is reported that one incident, Murtaza invited Zardari to his house, only to have him pinned down by bodyguards and half his moustache forcibly shaved off.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200826_208-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200826-208">[207]</a></sup> Not long after, Murtaza was ambushed and killed by police near Karachi.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200826Mu.C3.B1oz201366_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200826Mu.C3.B1oz201366-209">[208]</a></sup> It was widely believed in Pakistan that the killing had been ordered by a senior government figure.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia200826_208-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia200826-208">[207]</a></sup> Bhutto brought in Scotland Yard to investigate, partly to quell rumous that she had ordered the killing,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201366_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201366-210">[209]</a></sup> although the case remained unsolved.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201367_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201367-211">[210]</a></sup> After Murtaza's death, Bhutto re-established a relationship with her mother.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008112_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008112-212">[211]</a></sup></p>
<p>After Bhutto hinted that Leghari had been involved in her brother's death, relations between the two broke down.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008111_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008111-213">[212]</a></sup> Leghari sought the backing of the Army Chief, Karamat, to move against her premiership.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008111_213-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008111-213">[212]</a></sup> Leghari dismissed Bhutto's government on 5 November 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008111_213-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008111-213">[212]</a></sup> In the ensuing election, which took place in February 1997, Nawaz was re-elected.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008111Mu.C3.B1oz201368_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008111Mu.C3.B1oz201368-214">[213]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_dismissal">Second dismissal</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Second dismissal">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>On 20 July 1996, Qazi Hussain Ahmed of Jamaat e Islami announced to start protests against government alleging corruption. Qazi Hussain resigned from senate on 27 September and announced to start long march against Benazir government. Protest started on 27 October 1996 by Jamaat e Islami and opposition parties. On 4 November 1996, Bhutto's government was dismissed by President Leghari primarily because of corruption,<sup id="cite_ref-nyarrest_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nyarrest-215">[214]</a></sup> who used the Eighth Amendment discretionary powers to dissolve the government. Benazir was surprised when she discovered that it was not the military who had dismissed her but her own hand-picked puppet President who had used the power to dismiss her. She turned to the Supreme Court hoping for gaining Leghari's actions unconstitutional. But the Supreme Court justified and affirmed President Leghari's dismissal in a 6–1 ruling.<sup id="cite_ref-216" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-216">[215]</a></sup> Many military leaders who were close to Prime minister rather than the President, did not wanted Benazir Bhutto's government to fall, as they resisted the Nawaz Sharif's conservatism.<sup id="cite_ref-Mazhar_199-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mazhar-199">[198]</a></sup></p>
<p>Criticism against Benazir Bhutto came from the powerful political spectrum of the <a href="/wiki/Punjab_Province_(Pakistan)" class="mw-redirect" title="Punjab Province (Pakistan)">Punjab Province</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Azad_Kashmir" title="Azad Kashmir">Kashmir Province</a> who opposed Benazir Bhutto, particularly the nationalisation issue that led the loss of Punjab's privatised industries under the hands of her government. Bhutto blamed this opposition for the destabilisation of Pakistan.<sup id="cite_ref-NationBooks_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NationBooks-217">[216]</a></sup> Chairman of the Joint Chiefs of Staff Committee General Jehangir Karamat at one point intervened in the conflict between President and the Prime Minister, and urged Benazir Bhutto to focus on <a href="/wiki/Good_governance" title="Good governance">good governance</a> and her ambitious programme of making the country into a <a href="/wiki/Welfare_state" title="Welfare state">welfare state</a>, but the misconduct of her cabinet ministers continued and the corruption which she was unable to struck it down with a full force. Her younger brother's death had devastating effect on Benazir's party and policies. At one point, Chairman of Joint Chiefs General Jehangir Karamat noted that:<sup id="cite_ref-Mazhar_199-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mazhar-199">[198]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>In my opinion, if we have to repeat of past events then we must understand that Military leaders can pressure only up to a point. Beyond that their own position starts getting undermined because the military is after all is a mirror image of the society from which it is drawn.</p>
<div class="templatequotecite">— <cite>General Jehangir Karamat commenting on Benazir's dismissal</cite></div>
</blockquote>
<h2><span id="Second_term_as_leader_of_the_opposition_(1996–99)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Second_term_as_leader_of_the_opposition_.281996.E2.80.9399.29">Second term as leader of the opposition (1996–99)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Benazir_Bhutto&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Second term as leader of the opposition (1996–99)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Newly re-elected, Nawaz moved quickly to curtail the powers of the presidency and judiciary.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201368_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201368-218">[217]</a></sup> He also removed the constitution's Eighth Amendment which had been used by successive presidents to oust both himself and Bhutto from office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201368_218-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201368-218">[217]</a></sup> In 1998, Bhutto left Pakistan and settled into a self-exile in <a href="/wiki/Dubai" title="Dubai">Dubai</a>, a city in the <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">United Arab Emirates</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201368_218-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201368-218">[217]</a></sup> She decided on Dubai because <a href="/wiki/Zayed_bin_Sultan_Al_Nahyan" title="Zayed bin Sultan Al Nahyan">Zayed bin Sultan Al Nahyan</a>, the <a href="/wiki/President_of_the_United_Arab_Emirates" title="President of the United Arab Emirates">President of the United Arab Emirates</a>, had been a longstanding friend of her family.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008111.E2.80.93112_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008111.E2.80.93112-219">[218]</a></sup> She remained head of the PPP for the following nine years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201368_218-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201368-218">[217]</a></sup> It was from Dubai that Bhutto observed her country wage the 1999 <a href="/wiki/Kargil_War" title="Kargil War">Kargil War</a> with India; she described the conflict as "Pakistan's biggest blunder".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008110_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008110-220">[219]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bhutto spent much of her time in exile fighting the corruption charges that were being brought against her and her husband.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201368_218-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201368-218">[217]</a></sup> She also campaigned for his release from prison.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201368_218-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201368-218">[217]</a></sup> Some of her close political advisers suggested that she abandon him for the sake of her political career, but she refused.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201368_218-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201368-218">[217]</a></sup> In April 1999, the Ehtesab Bench of the Lahore Hugh Court convicted Bhutto and her husband <i>in absentia</i>, giving them a five years prison sentence each, an $8.6 million fine, and disqualifying them both from public office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201368.E2.80.9369_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201368.E2.80.9369-221">[220]</a></sup> Two years later a retrial was ordered after it was ascertained that undue political bias was exerted on the judges.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201369_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201369-222">[221]</a></sup></p>
<p>In October 1999, Musharraf launched a military coup which ousted Sharif from power.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201369_222-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201369-222">[221]</a></sup> In April 2000 Sharif was convicted of treason and sentenced to life imprisonment.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201370_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201370-223">[222]</a></sup> At the request of the Saudi monarch, Sharif was released from prison after a year and exiled to Saudi Arabia.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201370_223-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201370-223">[222]</a></sup> He was also legally disqualified from holding public office.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201370_223-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201370-223">[222]</a></sup> Musharraf called both Bhutto and Sharif "useless politicians".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201370_223-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201370-223">[222]</a></sup> In 2002, Musharraf amended the constitution to ban anyone serving more than two terms as Prime Minister, thus disqualifying both Bhutto and Sharif.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201370_223-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201370-223">[222]</a></sup> Musharraf also consolidated power around himself; in June 2001 he appointed himself to the Presidency, holding this position alongside his positions as chief executive and chief of the army staff.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201370_223-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201370-223">[222]</a></sup> It was also from Dubai that she observed the 2002 referendum through which Musharraf extended his presidential mandate for a further five years.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008110_220-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBhatia2008110-220">[219]</a></sup> Bhutto expressed concern that with mainstream politicians removed from Pakistan's political arena, the vacuum would be filled by Islamist extremists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201370_223-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMu.C3.B1oz201370-223">[222]</a></sup></p>
<p>Bhutto was highly confident that her party would secure an overwhelming victory in the coming Senate elections in 1999, due to the prime minister's widening unpopularity. Controversially, when the Pakistani armed forced initiated a coup d'état, Bhutto neither criticised nor issued any comment, remaining silent on supporting General Musharraf, as Dalrymple notes.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian-224">[223]</a></sup> She continued to support Musharraf's coordinated arrests of the supporters and staff of Sharif. Musharraf destroyed Sharif's political presence in Sindh and Kashmir provinces<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="Please clarify the following statement or statements with a good explanation from a reliable source. (May 2016)">how?</span></a></i>]</sup>. Many political offices in Sharif's constituency or district were forcibly closed and many sympathisers were jailed.
